‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ explicitly in the subscript here, since we are quantifying over it.
Note that, due to setting ‚ÉóW to ‚Éów, the values of the variables in ‚ÉóZ may change. AC2(bo)
says that this change does not affect œï; œï continues to be true. Indeed, œï continues to be true
even if some variables in ‚ÉóZ are forced to their original values.
Before going on, I need to make a brief technical digression to explain a slight abuse of
notation in AC2(bo). Suppose that ‚ÉóZ = (Z1, Z2), ‚Éóz = (1, 0), and ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ = (Z1). Then ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éóz
is intended to be an abbreviation for Z1 ‚Üê1; that is, I am ignoring the second component of
‚Éóz here. More generally, when I write ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éóz, I am picking out the values in ‚Éóz that correspond
to the variables in ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ and ignoring those that correspond to the variables in ‚ÉóZ ‚àí‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤. I similarly
write ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éów if ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ is a subset of ‚ÉóW.
For reasons that will become clearer in Example 2.8.1, the original deÔ¨Ånition was updated
to use a stronger version of AC2(bo). SufÔ¨Åciency is now required to hold if the variables in
any subset ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ of ‚ÉóW are set to the values in ‚Éów (as well as the variables in any subset ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ of
‚ÉóZ ‚àí‚ÉóX being set to their values in the actual context). Taking ‚ÉóZ and ‚ÉóW as in AC2(a) (and
AC2(bo)), here is the updated deÔ¨Ånition:
AC2(bu). If ‚Éóz‚àóis such that (M, ‚Éóu) |= ‚ÉóZ = ‚Éóz‚àó, then for all subsets ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ of ‚ÉóW and subsets ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤
of ‚ÉóZ ‚àí‚ÉóX, we have (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éów, ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éóz‚àó]œï.
In statisticians‚Äô notation, this becomes ‚Äúif ‚ÉóZ(‚Éóu) = ‚Éóz‚àó, then for all subsets ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ of ‚ÉóZ and ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤
of ‚ÉóW, we have ‚ÉóY ‚Éó
X‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚Éó
W ‚Ä≤‚Üê‚Éów,‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤‚Üê‚Éóz‚àó(‚Éóu) = ‚Éóy.‚Äù Again, we need to write the variables in the
subscript here.
The only difference between AC2(bo) and AC2(bu) lies in the clause ‚Äúfor all subsets ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤
of ‚ÉóW‚Äù: AC2(bu) must hold even if only a subset ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ of the variables in ‚ÉóW are set to their

2.2 A Formal DeÔ¨Ånition of Actual Cause
25
values in ‚Éów. This means that the variables in ‚ÉóW ‚àí‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ essentially act as they do in the real
world; that is, their values are determined by the structural equations, rather than being set to
their values in ‚Éów.
The superscripts o and u in AC2(bo) and AC2(bu) are intended to denote ‚Äúoriginal‚Äù and
‚Äúupdated‚Äù.
I conclude by considering the simpler modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition. This deÔ¨Ånition is motivated by
the intuition that only what happens in the actual situation should matter. Thus, the only
settings of variables allowed are ones that occur in the actual situation. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, the
modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition simpliÔ¨Åes AC2(a) by requiring that the only setting ‚Éów of the variables
in ‚ÉóW that can be considered is the value of these variables in the actual context. Here is the
modiÔ¨Åed AC2(a), denoted AC2(am) (the m stands for ‚ÄúmodiÔ¨Åed‚Äù):
AC2(am). There is a set ‚ÉóW of variables in V and a setting ‚Éóx‚Ä≤ of the variables in ‚ÉóX such that
if (M, ‚Éóu) |= ‚ÉóW = ‚Éów‚àó, then
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx‚Ä≤, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów‚àó]¬¨œï.
That is, we can show the counterfactual dependence of œï on ‚ÉóX by keeping the variables in ‚ÉóW
Ô¨Åxed at their actual values. In statisticians‚Äô notation (with œï being ‚ÉóY = ‚Éóy), this becomes ‚Äúif
‚ÉóW(‚Éóu) = ‚Éów‚àó, then ‚ÉóY‚Éóx‚Ä≤ ‚Éów‚àó(‚Éóu) Ã∏= ‚Éóy‚Äù.
It is easy to see that AC2(bo) holds if all the variables in ‚ÉóW are Ô¨Åxed at their values in the
actual context:
because ‚Éów‚àórecords the value of the variables in ‚ÉóW in the actual context, if
‚ÉóX is (re)set to ‚Éóx, its value in the actual context, then œï must hold (since, by AC1, ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx and
œï both hold in the actual context); that is, we have (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów‚àó]œï if ‚Éów‚àóis
the value of the variables in ‚ÉóW in the actual context.
For similar reasons, AC2(bu) holds if
all the variables in ‚ÉóW are Ô¨Åxed at their values in the actual context. Thus, there is no need
for an analogue to AC2(bo) or AC2(bu) in the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition. This shows that the need
for a sufÔ¨Åciency condition arises only if we are considering contingencies that differ from the
actual setting in AC2(a). It is also worth noting that the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition does not need to
mention ‚ÉóZ (although ‚ÉóZ can be taken to be the complement of ‚ÉóW).
For future reference, for all variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition, the tuple ( ‚ÉóW, ‚Éów, ‚Éóx‚Ä≤) in AC2 is
said to be a witness to the fact that ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï. (I take the witness to be (‚àÖ, ‚àÖ, ‚Éóx‚Ä≤)
in the special case that ‚ÉóW = ‚àÖ.)
As I said, AC3 is a minimality condition. Technically, just as there are three versions of
AC2, there are three corresponding versions of AC3. For example, in the case of the modiÔ¨Åed
deÔ¨Ånition, AC3 should really say ‚Äúthere is no subset of ‚ÉóX satisfying AC1 and AC2(am)‚Äù. I
will not bother writing out these versions of AC3; I hope that the intent is clear whenever I
refer to AC3.
If I need to specify which variant of the HP deÔ¨Ånition I am considering, I will say that
‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï according to the original (resp., updated, modiÔ¨Åed) HP deÔ¨Ånition. If
‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï according to all three variants, I often just say ‚Äú ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of
œï in (M, ‚Éóu)‚Äù. Each conjunct in ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is called part of a cause of œï in context (M, ‚Éóu). As
we shall see, what we think of as causes in natural language correspond to parts of causes,
especially with the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. Indeed, it may be better to use a term such as

26
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
‚Äúcomplete cause‚Äù for what I have been calling cause and then reserve ‚Äúcause‚Äù for what I have
called ‚Äúpart of a cause‚Äù. I return to this point after looking at a few examples.
Actually, although ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is an abbreviation for the conjunction X1 = x1‚àß. . .‚àßXk = xk,
when we talk about ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx being a cause of œï (particularly for the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition), it
might in some ways be better to think of the disjunction. Roughly speaking, ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause
of œï if it is the case that if none of the variables in ‚ÉóX had their actual value, then œï might not
have occurred. However, if ‚ÉóX has more than one variable, then just changing the value of a
single variable in ‚ÉóX (or, indeed, any strict subset of the variables in ‚ÉóX) is not enough by itself
to bring about ¬¨œï (given the context). Thus, there is a sense in which the disjunction can be
viewed as a but-for cause of œï. This will be clearer when we see the examples.
Note that all three variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition declare X = x a cause of itself in (M, u)
as long as (M, u) |= X = x. This does not seem to me so unreasonable. At any rate, it
seems fairly harmless, so I do not bother excluding it (although nothing would change if we
did exclude it).
At this point, ideally, I would prove a theorem showing that some variant of the HP def-
inition of actual causality is the ‚Äúright‚Äù deÔ¨Ånition of actual causality. But I know of no way
to argue convincingly that a deÔ¨Ånition is the ‚Äúright‚Äù one; the best we can hope to do is to
show that it is useful. As a Ô¨Årst step, I show that all the deÔ¨Ånitions agree in the simplest and
arguably most common case: but-for causes. Formally, say that X = x is a but-for cause of
œï in (M, ‚Éóu) if AC1 holds (so that (M, ‚Éóu) |= (X = x) ‚àßœï) and there exists some x‚Ä≤ such that
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [X ‚Üêx‚Ä≤]¬¨œï. Note here I am assuming that the cause is a single conjunct.
Proposition 2.2.2 If X = x is a but-for cause of Y = y in (M, ‚Éóu), then X = x is a cause of
Y = y according to all three variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition.
Proof:
Suppose that X = x is a but-for cause of Y = y. There must be a possible value
x‚Ä≤ of X such that (M, ‚Éóu) |= [X ‚Üêx‚Ä≤]¬¨œï. Then (‚àÖ, ‚àÖ, x‚Ä≤) (i.e., ‚ÉóW = ‚àÖand X = x‚Ä≤) is a
witness to X = x being a cause of œï for all three variants of the deÔ¨Ånition. Thus, AC2(a)
and AC2(am) hold if we take ‚ÉóW = ‚àÖ. Since (M, ‚Éóu) |= X = x, if (M, ‚Éóu) |= ‚ÉóZ = ‚Éóz‚àó, where
‚ÉóZ = V ‚àí{X}, then it is easy to see that (M, ‚Éóu) |= [X ‚Üêx](‚ÉóZ = ‚Éóz‚àó): since X = x and
‚ÉóZ = ‚Éóz‚àóin the (unique) solution to the equations in context ‚Éóu, this is still the case in the unique
solution to the equations in MX‚Üêx. For similar reasons, (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éóz‚àó]œï for
all subsets ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ of V ‚àí{X}. (See Lemma 2.10.2, where these statements are formalized.) Thus,
AC2(bo) holds. Because ‚ÉóW = ‚àÖ, AC2(bu) follows immediately from AC2(bo).
Of course, the deÔ¨Ånitions do not always agree. Other connections between the deÔ¨Ånitions
are given in the following theorem. In the statement of the theorem, the notation | ‚ÉóX| denotes
the cardinality of ‚ÉóX.

2.2 A Formal DeÔ¨Ånition of Actual Cause
27
Theorem 2.2.3
(a) If X = x is part of a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, then
X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original HP deÔ¨Ånition.
(b) If X = x is part of a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, then
X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition.
(c) If X = x is part of a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, then
X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original HP deÔ¨Ånition.
(d) If ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original HP deÔ¨Ånition, then | ‚ÉóX| = 1
(i.e., ‚ÉóX is a singleton).
Of course, (a) follows from (b) and (c); I state it separately just to emphasize the relations.
Although it may not look like it at Ô¨Årst glance, part (d) is quite similar in spirit to parts (a),
(b), and (c); an equivalent reformulation of (d) is: ‚ÄúIf X = x is part of a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu)
according to the original HP deÔ¨Ånition, then X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to
the original HP deÔ¨Ånition.‚Äù Call this statement (d‚Ä≤). Clearly (d) implies (d‚Ä≤). For the converse,
suppose that (d‚Ä≤) holds, ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu), and | ‚ÉóX| > 1. Then if X = x is a
conjunct of ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx, by (d‚Ä≤), X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu). But by AC3, this can hold only
if X = x is the only conjunct of ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx, so | ‚ÉóX| = 1.
Parts (a), (b), and (c) of Theorem 2.2.3 show that, in a sense, the original HP deÔ¨Ånition is
the most permissive of the three and the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition is the most restrictive, with
the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition lying somewhere in between. The converses of (a), (b), and (c)
do not hold. As Example 2.8.1 shows, if X = x is a cause of œï according to the original
HP deÔ¨Ånition, it is not, in general, part of a cause according to the modiÔ¨Åed or updated HP
deÔ¨Ånition. In this example, we actually do not want causality to hold, so the example can
be viewed as showing that the original deÔ¨Ånition is too permissive (although this claim is
not quite as clear as it may appear at Ô¨Årst; see the discussion in Section 2.8). Example 2.8.2
shows that part of a cause according to the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition need not be (part of) a cause
according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition.
The bottom line here is that, although these deÔ¨Ånitions often agree, they do not always
agree. It is typically on the most problematic examples on which they disagree. This is
perhaps not surprising given that the original deÔ¨Ånition was updated and modiÔ¨Åed precisely
to handle such examples. However, even in cases where the original deÔ¨Ånition seems to
give ‚Äúwrong‚Äù answers, there are often ways of dealing with the problem that do not require
modifying the deÔ¨Ånition. Moreover, the fact that causes are always singletons with the original
deÔ¨Ånition makes it attractive in some ways. The jury is still out on what the ‚Äúright‚Äù deÔ¨Ånition
of causality is. Although my current preference is the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, I will consider
all three deÔ¨Ånitions throughout the book. Despite the extra burden for the reader (for which I
apologize in advance!), I think doing so gives a deeper insight into the subtleties of causality.
The proof of Theorem 2.2.3 can be found in Section 2.10.1. I recommend that the interested
reader defer reading the proof until after going through the examples in the next section, since
they provide more intuition for the deÔ¨Ånitions.

2.3
Examples
Because I cannot prove a theorem showing that (some variant of) the HP deÔ¨Ånition is the
‚Äúright‚Äù deÔ¨Ånition of causality, all I can do to argue that the deÔ¨Ånition is reasonable is to
show how it works in some of the examples that have proved difÔ¨Åcult for other deÔ¨Ånitions to
handle. That is one of the goals of this section. I also give examples that illustrate the subtle
differences between the variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition. I start with a few basic examples mainly
intended to show how the deÔ¨Ånition works.
Example 2.3.1
For the forest-Ô¨Åre example, I consider two causal models, M c and M d, for
the conjunctive and disjunctive cases, respectively. These models were described earlier; the
endogenous variables are L, MD, and FF, and U is the only exogenous variable. In both
cases, I want to consider the context (1, 1), so the lightning strikes and the arsonist drops the
match. In the conjunctive model, both the lightning and the dropped match are but-for causes
of the forest Ô¨Åre; if either one had not occurred, the Ô¨Åre would not have happened. Hence, by
Proposition 2.2.2, both L = 1 and MD = 1 are causes of FF = 1 in (M c, (1, 1)) according
to all three variants of the deÔ¨Ånition. By AC3, it follows that L = 1 ‚àßMD = 1 is not a cause
of FF = 1 in (M c, (1, 1)). This already shows that causes might not be unique; there may be
more than one cause of a given outcome.
However, in the disjunctive case, there are differences. With the original and updated
deÔ¨Ånition, again, we have that both L = 1 and MD = 1 are causes of FF = 1 in (M d, (1, 1)).
I give the argument in the case of L = 1; the argument for MD = 1 is identical. Clearly
(M d, (1, 1)) |= FF = 1 and (M d, (1, 1)) |= L = 1; in the context (1, 1), the lightning
strikes and the forest burns down. Thus, AC1 is satisÔ¨Åed. AC3 is trivially satisÔ¨Åed: since ‚ÉóX
consists of only one element, L, ‚ÉóX must be minimal.
For AC2, as suggested earlier, let ‚ÉóZ = {L, FF}, ‚ÉóW = {MD}, x‚Ä≤ = 0, and w = 0.
Clearly, (M d, (1, 1)) |= [L ‚Üê0, MD ‚Üê0](FF Ã∏= 1); if the lightning does not strike and the
match is not dropped, the forest does not burn down, so AC2(a) is satisÔ¨Åed. To see the effect
of the lightning, we must consider the contingency where the match is not dropped; AC2(a)
allows us to do that by setting MD to 0. (Note that setting L and MD to 0 overrides the effects
of U; this is critical.) Moreover,
(M d, (1, 1)) |= [L ‚Üê1, MD ‚Üê0](FF = 1) and (M d, (1, 1)) |= [L ‚Üê1](FF = 1);
in context (1, 1), if the lightning strikes, then the forest burns down even if the lit match is
not dropped, so AC2(bo) and AC2(bu) are satisÔ¨Åed. (Note that since ‚ÉóZ = {L, FF}, the only
subsets of ‚ÉóZ ‚àí‚ÉóX are the empty set and the singleton set consisting of just FF; similarly, since
‚ÉóW = {MD}, the only subsets of ‚ÉóW are the empty set and the singleton set consisting of MD
itself. Thus, we have considered all the relevant cases here.)
This argument fails in the case of the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition because the setting MD = 0 is
not allowed. The only witnesses that can be considered are ones where ‚ÉóW has the value it does
in the actual context; in the actual context here, MD = 1. So, with the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition,
neither L = 1 nor MD = 1 is a cause. However, it is not hard to see that L = 1 ‚àßMD = 1
is a cause of FF = 1. This shows why it is critical to consider only single conjuncts in
Proposition 2.2.2 and Theorem 2.2.3 is worded in terms of parts of causes. Although L =

2.3 Examples
29
1 ‚àßMD = 1 is a cause of FF = 1 according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, it is not a cause
according to either the original or updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. In contrast, L = 1 and MD = 1 are
both parts of a cause of FF = 1 according to all three deÔ¨Ånitions.
It is arguably a feature of the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition that they call both L = 1
and MD = 1 causes of FF = 1. Calling the conjunction L = 1 ‚àßMD = 1 a cause of
FF = 1 does not seem to accord with natural language usage. There are two ways to address
this concern. As I suggested earlier, with the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, it may be better to think
of parts of causes as coming closer to what we call causes in natural language. That would
already deal with this concern. A second approach, which I also hinted at earlier, is to observe
that it may be better to think of the disjunction L = 1 ‚à®MD = 1 as being the cause. Indeed,
we can think of MD = 1 ‚à®L = 1 as a but-for cause of FF = 1; if it does not hold, then it
must be the case both L = 0 and MD = 0, so there is no Ô¨Åre. The reader should keep in mind
both of these ways of thinking of conjunctive causes with the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition.
As we shall see, the notion of responsibility, discussed in Chapter 6, allows the original
and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition to distinguish these two scenarios, as does the notion of sufÔ¨Åcient
cause, discussed in Section 2.6.
This simple example already reveals some of the power of the HP deÔ¨Ånition. The case
where the forest Ô¨Åre is caused by either the lightning or the dropped match (this is said to be a
case of overdetermination in the literature) cannot be handled by the simple but-for deÔ¨Ånition
used in the law. It seems reasonable to call both the lightning and the dropped match causes,
or at least parts of causes. We certainly wouldn‚Äôt want to say that there is no cause, as a
naive application of the but-for deÔ¨Ånition would do. (However, as I noted above, if we allow
disjunctive causes, then a case can be made that the disjunction L = 1 ‚à®MD = 1 is a but-for
cause of the forest Ô¨Åre.) Overdetermination occurs frequently in legal cases; a victim may
be shot by two people, for example. It occurs in voting as well; I look at this a little more
carefully in the following example.
Example 2.3.2 Consider the voting scenario discussed earlier where there are 11 voters. If
Suzy wins 6‚Äì5, then all the deÔ¨Ånitions agree that each of the voters for Suzy is a cause of
Suzy‚Äôs victory; indeed, they are all but-for causes. But suppose that Suzy wins 11‚Äì0. Here
we have overdetermination. The original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition would still call each of
the voters a cause of Suzy winning; the witness involves switching the votes of 5 voters.
Since the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition does not allow such switching, according to the modiÔ¨Åed
HP deÔ¨Ånition, any subset of six voters is a cause of Suzy winning (and every individual is part
of a cause). Again, if we think of the subset as being represented by a disjunction, it can be
thought of as a but-for cause of Suzy winning. If all six voters had switched their votes to
Billy, then Suzy would not have won. Minimality holds; if a set of fewer than six voters had
voted for Billy, then Suzy would still have won.
We do have an intuition that a voter for Suzy is somehow ‚Äúless‚Äù of a cause of the victory
in an 11‚Äì0 victory than in a 6‚Äì5 victory. This intuition is perhaps most compatible with the
modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. In this case, ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï if ‚ÉóX is a minimal set of variables
whose values have to change for ¬¨œï to hold. The bigger ‚ÉóX is, the less of a cause each of its
conjuncts is. I return to this issue when I discuss responsibility in Chapter 6.

30
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
Example 2.3.3 Now I (Ô¨Ånally!) consider the rock-throwing example, where Suzy and Billy
both throw rocks at a bottle, but Suzy‚Äôs hits the bottle, and Billy‚Äôs doesn‚Äôt (although it would
have hit had Suzy‚Äôs not hit Ô¨Årst). We get the desired result‚Äîthat Suzy‚Äôs throw is a cause, but
Billy‚Äôs is not‚Äîbut only if we model the story appropriately. Consider Ô¨Årst a coarse causal
model, with three endogenous variables:
ST for ‚ÄúSuzy throws‚Äù, with values 0 (Suzy does not throw) and 1 (she does);
BT for ‚ÄúBilly throws‚Äù, with values 0 (he doesn‚Äôt) and 1 (he does);
BS for ‚Äúbottle shatters‚Äô, with values 0 (it doesn‚Äôt shatter) and 1 (it does).
For simplicity, assume that there is one exogenous variable u, which determines whether Billy
and Suzy throw. (In most of the subsequent examples, I omit the exogenous variable in both
the description of the story and the corresponding causal network.) Take the formula for BS
to be such that the bottle shatters if either Billy or Suzy throws; that is, BS = BT ‚à®ST. (I
am implicitly assuming that Suzy and Billy never miss if they throw. Also, I follow the fairly
standard mathematics convention of saying ‚Äúif‚Äù rather than ‚Äúif and only if‚Äù in deÔ¨Ånitions;
clearly, the equation is saying that the bottle shatters if and only if either Billy or Suzy throws.)
For future reference, I call this model MRT (where RT stands for ‚Äúrock throwing‚Äù).
BT and ST play symmetric roles in MRT; there is nothing to distinguish them. Not sur-
prisingly, both Billy‚Äôs throw and Suzy‚Äôs throw are classiÔ¨Åed as causes of the bottle shattering
in MRT in the context u where Suzy and Billy both throw according to the original and up-
dated HP deÔ¨Ånition (the conjunction ST = 1‚àßBT = 1 is the cause according to the modiÔ¨Åed
HP deÔ¨Ånition). The argument is essentially identical to that used for the disjunctive model of
the forest-Ô¨Åre example, where either the lightning or the dropped match is enough to start the
Ô¨Åre. Indeed, the causal network describing this situation looks like that in Figure 2.1, with ST
and BT replacing L and MD. For convenience, I redraw the network here. As I said earlier,
here and in later Ô¨Ågures, I typically omit the exogenous variable(s).

The trouble with MRT is that it cannot distinguish the case where both rocks hit the bottle
simultaneously (in which case it would be reasonable to say that both ST = 1 and BT = 1
are causes of BS = 1) from the case where Suzy‚Äôs rock hits Ô¨Årst. MRT has to be reÔ¨Åned
to express this distinction. One way is to invoke a dynamic model. Although this can be
done (see the notes at the end of the chapter for more discussion), a simpler way to gain
the expressiveness needed here is to allow BS to be three-valued, with values 0 (the bottle
doesn‚Äôt shatter), 1 (it shatters as a result of being hit by Suzy‚Äôs rock), and 2 (it shatters as a
result of being hit by Billy‚Äôs rock). I leave it to the reader to check that ST = 1 is a cause

2.3 Examples
31
of BS = 1, but BT = 1 is not (if Suzy doesn‚Äôt throw but Billy does, then we would have
BS = 2). To some extent, this solves our problem. But it borders on cheating; the answer is
almost programmed into the model by invoking the relation ‚Äúas a result of‚Äù in the deÔ¨Ånition
of BS = 2, which requires the identiÔ¨Åcation of the actual cause.
A more useful choice is to add two new variables to the model:
BH for ‚ÄúBilly‚Äôs rock hits the (intact) bottle‚Äù, with values 0 (it doesn‚Äôt) and 1 (it does);
and
SH for ‚ÄúSuzy‚Äôs rock hits the bottle‚Äù, again with values 0 and 1.
We now modify the equations as follows:
BS = 1 if SH = 1 or BH = 1;
SH = 1 if ST = 1;
BH = 1 if BT = 1 and SH = 0.
Thus, Billy‚Äôs throw hits if Billy throws and Suzy‚Äôs rock doesn‚Äôt hit. The last equation implic-
itly assumes that Suzy throws slightly ahead of Billy, or slightly harder.
Call this model M ‚Ä≤
RT. M ‚Ä≤

fact that Billy‚Äôs throw doesn‚Äôt hit the bottle if Suzy throws) is modeled by the fact that there
is an edge from SH to BH but not one in the other direction; BH depends (in part) on SH
but not vice versa.

Taking u to be the context where Billy and Suzy both throw, according to all three variants

see that ST = 1 is a cause according to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, note that it
is immediate that AC1 and AC3 hold. To see that AC2 holds, one possibility is to choose
‚ÉóZ = {ST, SH , BH , BS}, ‚ÉóW = {BT}, and w = 0. When BT is set to 0, BS tracks ST:
if Suzy throws, the bottle shatters, and if she doesn‚Äôt throw, the bottle does not shatter. It
immediately follows that AC2(a) and AC2(bo) hold. AC2(bu) is equivalent to AC2(bo) in this
case, since ‚ÉóW is a singleton.

Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
To see that BT = 1 is not a cause of BS = 1 in (M ‚Ä≤
RT, u), we must check that there is no
partition of the endogenous variables into sets ‚ÉóZ and ‚ÉóW that satisÔ¨Åes AC2. Attempting the
symmetric choice with ‚ÉóZ = {BT, BH , SH BS}, ‚ÉóW = {ST}, and w = 0 violates AC2(bo)
and AC2(bu). To see this, take ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ = {BH }. In the context where Suzy and Billy both throw,
BH = 0. If BH is set to 0, then the bottle does not shatter if Billy throws and Suzy does
not. It is precisely because, in this context, Suzy‚Äôs throw hits the bottle and Billy‚Äôs does not
that we declare Suzy‚Äôs throw to be the cause of the bottle shattering. AC2(bo) and AC2(bu)
capture that intuition by forcing us to consider the contingency where BH = 0 (i.e., where
BH takes on its actual value), despite the fact that Billy throws.
Of course, just checking that this particular partition into ‚ÉóZ and ‚ÉóW does not make BT = 1
a cause does not sufÔ¨Åce to establish that BT = 1 is not a cause according to the original or
updated deÔ¨Ånitions; we must check all possible partitions. I just sketch the argument here:
The key is to consider whether BH is in ‚ÉóW or ‚ÉóZ. If BH is in ‚ÉóW, then how we set BT has
no effect on the value of BS: If BH is set to 0, then the value of BS is determined by the
value of ST; and if BH is set to 1, then BS = 1 no matter what BT is. (It may seem strange
to intervene and set BH to 1 if BT = 0. It means that somehow Billy hits the bottle even if
he doesn‚Äôt throw. Such ‚Äúmiraculous‚Äù interventions are allowed by the deÔ¨Ånition; part of the
motivation for the notion of normality considered in Chapter 3 is to minimize their usage.)
This shows that we cannot have BH in ‚ÉóW. If BH is in ‚ÉóZ, then we get the same problem with
AC2(bo) or AC2(bu) as above; it is easy to see that at least one of SH or ST must be in ‚ÉóW,
and ‚Éów must be such that whichever is in ‚ÉóW is set to 0.
Note that, in this argument, it is critical that in AC2(bo) and AC2(bu) we allow setting an
arbitrary subset of variables in ‚ÉóZ ‚àí‚ÉóX to their original values. There is a trivial reason for this:
if we set all variables in ‚ÉóZ ‚àí‚ÉóX to their original values in M ‚Ä≤
RT, then, among other things, we
will set BS to 1. We will never be able to show that Billy‚Äôs throw is not a cause if BS is set
to 1. But even requiring that all the variables in ‚ÉóZ ‚àí{BS, BT} be set to their original values
does not work. For suppose that we take ‚ÉóW = {ST} and take ‚Éów such that ST = 0. Clearly
AC2(a) holds because if BT = 0, then BS = 0. But if all the variables in ‚ÉóZ ‚àí{BT, BS} are
set to their original values, then SH is set to 1, so the bottle shatters. To show that BT = 1
is not a cause, we must be able to set BH to its original value of 0 while keeping SH at 0.
Setting BH to 0 captures the intuition that Billy‚Äôs throw is not a cause because, in the actual
world, his rock did not hit the bottle (BH = 0).
Finally, consider the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. Here things are much simpler. In this case,
taking ‚ÉóW = {BT} does not work to show that ST = 1 is a cause of BS = 1; we are
not allowed to change BT from 1 to 0. However, we can take ‚ÉóW = {BH }. Fixing BH
at 0 (its setting in the actual context), if ST is set to 0, then BS = 0, that is, (M, u) |=
[ST ‚Üê0, BH ‚Üê0](BS = 0). Thus, ST = 1 is a cause of BS = 1 according to the modiÔ¨Åed
HP deÔ¨Ånition. (Taking ‚ÉóW = {BH } would also have worked to show that ST = 1 is a cause
of BS = 1 according to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. Indeed, by Theorem 2.2.3,
showing that ST = 1 is a cause of BS = 1 according to the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition sufÔ¨Åces to
show that it is also a cause according to the original and updated deÔ¨Ånition. I went through
the argument for the original and updated deÔ¨Ånition because the more obvious choice for ‚ÉóW
in that case is {BT}, and it works under those deÔ¨Ånitions.) But now it is also easy to check
that BT = 1 is not a cause of BS = 1 according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. No matter

which subset of variables other than BT are held to their values in u and no matter how we
set BT, we have SH = 1 and thus BS = 1.
I did not completely specify M ‚Ä≤
RT in the example above. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, I did not specify

context where Billy and Suzy both throw, implicitly suggesting that there are four contexts,
determining the four possible combinations of Billy throwing and Suzy throwing. So we can
take MRT ‚Ä≤ to be the model with these four contexts.
There is arguably a problem with this deÔ¨Ånition of M ‚Ä≤
RT: it ‚Äúbakes in‚Äù the temporal order-
ing of events, in particular, that Suzy‚Äôs rock hits before Billy‚Äôs rock. If we want a model that
describes ‚Äúrock-throwing situations for Billy and Suzy‚Äù more generally, we would not want
to necessarily assume that Billy and Suzy are accurate, nor that Suzy always hits before Billy
if they both throw. Whatever assumptions we make along these lines can be captured by the
context. That is, we can have the context determine (a) whether Suzy and Billy throw, (b) how
accurate they are (would Billy hit if he were the only one to throw, and similarly for Suzy),
and (c) whose rock hits Ô¨Årst if they both throw and are accurate (allowing for the possibility
that they both hit at the same time). This richer model would thus have 48 contexts: there are
four choices of which subset of Billy and Suzy throw, four possibilities for accuracy (both are
accurate, Suzy is accurate and Billy is not, and so on), and three choices regarding who hits
Ô¨Årst if they both throw. If we use this richer model, we would also want to expand the lan-
guage to include variables such as SA (Suzy is accurate), BA (Billy is accurate), SF (Suzy‚Äôs
throw arrives Ô¨Årst if both Billy and Suzy throw), and BF (Bily‚Äôs throw arrives Ô¨Årst). Call this
richer model M ‚àó
RT. In M ‚àó
RT , the values of ST, BT, SA, BA, SF, and BF are determined
by the context. As with MRT and M ‚Ä≤

we have the equation BS = BH ‚à®SH . But now the equations for BH and SH depend on
ST, BT, SA, BA, SF, and BF. Of course SH = 0 if Suzy doesn‚Äôt throw (i.e., if ST = 0)
or is inaccurate (SA = 0), but even in this case, the equations need to tell us what would have
happened had Suzy thrown and been accurate, so that we can determine the truth of formulas
such as [ST ‚Üê1, SA ‚Üê1](SH = 0). As suggested by the discussion above, the equation
for SH is
SH =

It is critical in this analysis of the rock-throwing example that the model includes the
variables BH and SH , that is, that Billy‚Äôs rock hitting the bottle is taken to be a different
event than Suzy‚Äôs rock hitting the bottle (although there is no problem if it includes extra
variables). To understand the need for BH and SH (or some analogous variables), consider
an observer watching the situation. Why would she declare Suzy‚Äôs throw to be a cause and
Billy‚Äôs throw not to be a cause? Presumably, precisely because it was Suzy‚Äôs rock that hit the
bottle and not Billy‚Äôs. If this is the reason for the declaration, then it must be modeled. Of
course, using the variables BH and SH is not the only way of doing this. All that is needed
is that the language be rich enough to allow us to distinguish the situation where Suzy‚Äôs rock
hits and Billy‚Äôs rock does not from the one where Billy‚Äôs rock hits and Suzy‚Äôs rock does
not. (That is why I said ‚Äúor some analogous variables‚Äù above.) An alternative approach to
incorporating temporal information is to have time-indexed variables (e.g., to have a family
of variables BS k for ‚Äúbottle shatters at time k‚Äù and a family Hk for ‚Äúbottle is hit at time k‚Äù).
With these variables and the appropriate equations, we can dispense with SH and BH and
just have the variables H1, H2, . . . that talk about the bottle being hit at time k, without the
variable specifying who hit the bottle. For example, if we assume that all the action happens
at times 1, 2, and 3, then the equations are H1 = ST (if Suzy throws, then the bottle is hit at
time 1), H2 = BT ‚àß¬¨H1 (the bottle is hit at time 2 if Billy throws and the bottle was not
already hit at time 1), and BS = H1 ‚à®H2 (if the bottle is hit, then it shatters). Again, these
equations model the fact that Suzy hits Ô¨Årst if she throws. And again, we could assume that
the equations for H1 and H2 are context-dependent, so that Suzy hits Ô¨Årst in u, but Billy hits
Ô¨Årst in u‚Ä≤. In any case, in context u where Suzy and Billy both throw but Suzy‚Äôs rock arrives
at the bottle Ô¨Årst, Suzy is still the cause, and Billy isn‚Äôt, using essentially the same analysis as
that above.
To summarize the key point here, the language (i.e., the variables chosen and the equations
for them) must be rich enough to capture the signiÔ¨Åcant features of the story, but there is more
than one language that can do this. As we shall see in Chapter 4, the choice of language can
in general have a major impact on causality judgments.
The rock-throwing example emphasizes an important moral. If we want to argue in a case
of preemption that X = x rather than Y = y is the cause of œï, then there must be a variable
(BH in this case) that takes on different values depending on whether X = x or Y = y is
the actual cause. If the model does not contain such a variable, then it will not be possible to
determine which one is in fact the cause. The need for such variables is certainly consistent
with intuition and the way we present evidence. If we want to argue (say, in a court of law)
that it was A‚Äôs shot that killed C and not B‚Äôs, then, assuming that A shot from C‚Äôs left and
B from the right, we would present evidence such as the bullet entering C from the left side.
The side from which the shot entered is the relevant variable in this case. The variable may
involve temporal evidence (if C‚Äôs shot had been the lethal one, then the death would have
occurred a few seconds later), but it certainly does not have to.
The rock-throwing example also emphasizes the critical role of what happens in the actual
situation in the deÔ¨Ånition. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, we make use of the fact that, in the actual context,

2.3 Examples
35
Billy‚Äôs rock did not hit the bottle. In the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, this fact is applied
in AC2(b): to show that Billy is a cause, we would have to show that the bottle shatters when
Billy throws, even if BH is set to its actual value of 0. In the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, it is
applied in AC2(am) to show that Suzy‚Äôs throw is a cause: we can set ST = 0 while keeping
BH Ô¨Åxed at 0. The analogous argument would not work to show that Billy‚Äôs throw is a cause.
Although what happens in the actual context certainly affects people‚Äôs causality judgments,
why is the way that this is captured in AC2(am), AC2(bo), or AC2(bu) the ‚Äúright‚Äù way of cap-
turing it? I do not have a compelling answer to this question, beyond showing that these
deÔ¨Ånitions work well in examples. I have tried to choose examples that, although not always
realistic, capture important features of causality. For example, while the rock-throwing ex-
ample may not seem so important in and of itself, it is an abstraction of a situation that arises
frequently in legal cases, where one potential cause is preempted by another. Monopolistic
practices by a big company cause a small company to go bankrupt, but it would have gone
bankrupt anyway because of poor management; a smoker dies in a car accident, but he would
have died soon due to inoperable lung cancer had there not been an accident. A good def-
inition of causality is critical for teasing out what is a cause and what is not in such cases.
Many of the other examples in this section are also intended to capture the essence of other
important issues that arise in legal (and everyday) reasoning. It is thus worth understanding
in all these cases exactly what the role of the actual context is.
Example 2.3.4 This example considers the problem of what has been called double preven-
tion.
Suzy and Billy have grown up just in time to get involved in World War III. Suzy
is piloting a bomber on a mission to blow up an enemy target, and Billy is piloting
a Ô¨Åghter as her lone escort. Along comes an enemy Ô¨Åghter plane, piloted by
Enemy. Sharp-eyed Billy spots Enemy, zooms in, and pulls the trigger; Enemy‚Äôs
plane goes down in Ô¨Çames. Suzy‚Äôs mission is undisturbed, and the bombing takes
place as planned.
Is Billy a cause of the success of the mission? After all, he prevented Enemy from prevent-
ing Suzy from carrying out the mission. Intuitively, it seems that the answer is yes, and the
obvious causal model gives us this. Suppose that we have the following variables:
BPT for ‚ÄúBilly pulls trigger‚Äù, with values 0 (he doesn‚Äôt) and 1 (he does);
EE for ‚ÄúEnemy eludes Billy‚Äù, with values 0 (he doesn‚Äôt) and 1 (he does);
ESS for ‚ÄúEnemy shoots Suzy‚Äù, with values 0 (he doesn‚Äôt) and 1 (he does);
SBT for ‚ÄúSuzy bombs target‚Äù, with values 0 (she doesn‚Äôt) and 1 (she does);
TD for ‚Äútarget destroyed‚Äù, with values 0 (it isn‚Äôt) and 1 (it is).
The causal network corresponding to this model is given in Figure 2.4.
In this model, BPT = 1 is a but-for cause of TD = 1, as is SBT = 1, so both BPT = 1
and SBT = 1 are causes of TD = 1 according to all the variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition.

So far, this may all seem reasonable. But now go back to the original story and suppose
that, again, Billy goes up along with Suzy, but Enemy does not actually show up. Is Billy
going up a cause of the target being destroyed? Clearly, if Enemy had shown up, then Billy
would have shot him down, and he would have been a but-for cause of the target being de-
stroyed. But what about the context where Enemy does not show up? It seems that the original
and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition would say that, even in that context, Billy going up is a cause of
the target being destroyed. For if we consider the contingency where Enemy had shown up,
then the target would not have been destroyed if Billy weren‚Äôt there, but since he is, the target
is destroyed.
This may seem disconcerting. Suppose it is known that just about all the enemy‚Äôs aircraft
have been destroyed, and no one has been coming up for days. Is Billy going up still a cause?
The concern is that, if so, almost any A can become a cause of any B by telling a story of how
there might have been a C that, but for A, would have prevented B from happening (just as
there might have been an Enemy that prevented the target being destroyed had Billy not been
there).
Does the HP deÔ¨Ånition really declare Billy showing up a cause of the target being destroyed
in this case? Let‚Äôs look a little more carefully at how we model this story. If we want to call

Billy going up a cause, then we need to add to the model a variable BGU corresponding to
Billy going up. Similarly, we should add a variable ESU corresponding to Enemy showing
up. We have the obvious equations, which say that (in the contexts of interest) BPT = 1
(i.e., Billy pulls the trigger) only if Billy goes up and Enemy shows up, and EE = 0 only
if either Enemy doesn‚Äôt show up or Billy pulls the trigger. This gives us the model shown in

In the actual world, where Billy goes up and Enemy doesn‚Äôt, Billy doesn‚Äôt pull the trigger

not a cause of the target being destroyed if Enemy doesn‚Äôt show up. AC2(bo) fails (and hence
so does AC2(bu)): if Enemy shows up, the target won‚Äôt be destroyed, even if Billy shows up,

even more straightforwardly. No matter which variables we Ô¨Åx to their actual values, TD = 1

Cases of prevention and double prevention are quite standard in the real world. It is stan-
dard to install Ô¨Åre alarms, which, of course, are intended to prevent Ô¨Åres. When the batteries
in a Ô¨Åre alarm become weak, Ô¨Åre alarms typically ‚Äúchirp‚Äù. Deactivating the chirping sound
could be a cause of a Ô¨Åre not being detected due to double prevention: deactivating the chirp-
ing sound prevents the Ô¨Åre alarm from sounding, which in turn prevents the Ô¨Åre from being
detected.
Example 2.3.5
Can not performing an action be (part of) a cause? Consider the following
story.
Billy, having stayed out in the cold too long throwing rocks, contracts a serious
but nonfatal disease. He is hospitalized and treated on Monday, so is Ô¨Åne Tuesday
morning.
But now suppose that the doctor does not treat Billy on Monday. Is the doctor‚Äôs not treating
Billy a cause of Billy‚Äôs being sick on Tuesday? It seems that it should be, and indeed it is
according to all variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition. Suppose that ‚Éóu is the context where, among
other things, Billy is sick on Monday and the doctor forgets to administer the medication
Monday. It seems reasonable that the model should have two variables:

38
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
MT for ‚ÄúMonday treatment‚Äù, with values 0 (the doctor does not treat Billy on Monday)
and 1 (he does); and
BMC for ‚ÄúBilly‚Äôs medical condition‚Äù, with values 0 (recovered) and 1 (still sick).
Sure enough, in the obvious causal setting, MT = 0 is a but-for cause of BMC = 1, and
hence a cause according to all three variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition.
This may seem somewhat disconcerting at Ô¨Årst. Suppose there are 100 doctors in the
hospital. Although only one of them was assigned to Billy (and he forgot to give medication),
in principle, any of the other 99 doctors could have given Billy his medication. Is the fact that
they didn‚Äôt give him the medication also part of the cause of him still being sick on Tuesday?
In the causal model above, the other doctors‚Äô failure to give Billy his medication is not a
cause, since the model has no variables to model the other doctors‚Äô actions, just as there was
no variable in the causal model of Example 2.3.1 to model the presence of oxygen. Their lack
of action is part of the context. We factor it out because (quite reasonably) we want to focus
on the actions of Billy‚Äôs doctor.
If we had included endogenous variables corresponding to the other doctors, then they too
would be causes of Billy‚Äôs being sick on Tuesday. The more reÔ¨Åned deÔ¨Ånition of causal-
ity given in Chapter 3, which takes normality into account, provides a way of avoiding this
problem even if the model includes endogenous variables for the other doctors.
Causation by omission is a major issue in the law. To take just one of many examples, a
surgeon can be sued for the harm caused due to a surgical sponge that he did not remove after
an operation.
The next example again emphasizes how the choice of model can change what counts as a
cause.
Example 2.3.6 The engineer is standing by a switch in the railroad tracks. A train approaches
in the distance. She Ô¨Çips the switch, so that the train travels down the right track instead of the
left. Because the tracks reconverge up ahead, the train arrives at its destination all the same.
If we model this story using three variables‚ÄîF for ‚ÄúÔ¨Çip‚Äù, with values 0 (the engineer
doesn‚Äôt Ô¨Çip the switch) and 1 (she does); T for ‚Äútrack‚Äù, with values 0 (the train goes on the
left track) and 1 (it goes on the right track); and A for ‚Äúarrival‚Äù, with values 0 (the train does
not arrive at the point of reconvergence) and 1 (it does)‚Äîthen all three deÔ¨Ånitions agree that
Ô¨Çipping the switch is not a cause of the train arriving.
But now suppose that we replace T with two binary variables, LB (for left track blocked),
which is 0 if the left track is not blocked, and 1 if it is, and RB (for right track blocked),
deÔ¨Åned symmetrically. Suppose that LB, RB, and F are determined by the context, while
the value of A is determined in the obvious way by which track the train is going down
(which is determined by how the switch is set) and whether the track that it is going down
is blocked; speciÔ¨Åcally, A = (F ‚àß¬¨LB) ‚à®(¬¨F ‚àß¬¨RB). In the actual context, F = 1 and
LB = RB = 0. Under the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, F = 1 is a cause of A = 1.
For in the contingency where LB = 1, if F = 1, then the train arrives, whereas if F = 0,
then the train does not arrive. While adding the variables LB and RB suggests that we care
about whether a track is blocked, it seems strange to call Ô¨Çipping the switch a cause of the
train arriving when in fact both tracks are unblocked. This problem can be dealt with to some

2.3 Examples
39
extent by invoking normality considerations, but not completely (see Example 3.4.3). With
the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition, the problem disappears. Flipping the switch is not a cause of the train
arriving if both tracks are unblocked, nor is it a cause of the train not arriving if both tracks
are blocked.
Example 2.3.7
Suppose that a captain and a sergeant stand before a private, both shout
‚ÄúCharge!‚Äù at the same time, and the private charges. Some have argued that, because orders
from higher-ranking ofÔ¨Åcers trump those of lower-ranking ofÔ¨Åcers, the captain is a cause of
the charge, whereas the sergeant is not.
It turns out that which of the sergeant or captain is a cause depends in part on the variant
of the HP deÔ¨Ånition we consider and in part on what the possible actions of the sergeant and
captain are. First, suppose that the sergeant and the captain can each either order an advance,
order a retreat, or do nothing. Formally, let C and S represent the captain‚Äôs and sergeant‚Äôs
order, respectively. Then C and S can each take three values, 1, ‚àí1, or 0, depending on the
order (attack, retreat, no order). The actual value of C and S is determined by the context. P
describes what the private does; as the story suggests, P = C if C Ã∏= 0; otherwise P = S. In
the actual context, C = S = P = 1.
C = 1 is a but-for cause of P = 1; setting C to ‚àí1 would result in P = ‚àí1. Thus, C = 1
is a cause of P = 1 according to all variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition. S = 1 is not a cause of
B = 1 according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition; changing S to 0 while keeping C Ô¨Åxed at
1 will not affect the private‚Äôs action. However, S = 1 is a cause of P = 1 according to the
original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, with witness ({C}, 0, 0) (i.e., in the witness world, C = 0
and S = 0): if the captain does nothing, then what the private does is completely determined
by the sergeant‚Äôs action.
Next, suppose that the captain and sergeant can only order an attack or a retreat; that is,
the range of each of C and S is {‚àí1, 1}. Now it is easy to check that C = 1 is the only
cause of P = 1 according to all the variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition. For the original and updated
deÔ¨Ånition, S = 1 is not a cause because there is no setting of C that will allow the sergeant‚Äôs
action to make a difference.
Finally, suppose that the captain and sergeant can only order an attack or do nothing; that is,
the range of each of C and S is {0, 1}. C = 1 and S = 1 are both causes of P = 1 according
to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, using the same argument used to show that S = 1
was a cause when it was also possible to order a retreat. Neither is a cause according to the
modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, but C = 1 ‚àßS = 1 is a cause, so both C = 1 and S = 1 are parts of
a cause.
Note that if the range of the variables C and S is {0, 1}, then there is no way to capture the
fact that in the case of conÔ¨Çicting orders, the private obeys the captain. In this setting, we can
capture the fact that the private is ‚Äúreally‚Äù obeying the captain if both the captain and sergeant
give orders by adding a new variable SE that captures the sergeant‚Äôs ‚Äúeffective‚Äù order. If the
captain does not issue any orders (i.e., if C = 0), then SE = S. If the captain does issue
an order, then SE = 0; the sergeant‚Äôs order is effectively blocked. In this model, P = C if
C Ã∏= 0; otherwise, P = SE. The causal network for this model is given in Figure 2.7.
In this model, the captain causes the private to advance, but the sergeant does not, according
to all variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition. To see that the captain is a cause according to the modiÔ¨Åed
HP deÔ¨Ånition, hold SE Ô¨Åxed at 0 and set C = 0; clearly P = 0. By Theorem 2.2.3, C = 1 is

P = 0, not 1. The key point is that this more reÔ¨Åned model allows a setting where C = 0,
S = 1, and P = 0 (because SE = 0). That is, despite the sergeant issuing an order to attack
and the captain being silent, the private does nothing.
The Ô¨Ånal example in this section touches on issues of responsibility in the law.
Example 2.3.8 Suppose that two companies both dump pollutant into the river. Company A
dumps 100 kilograms of pollutant; company B dumps 60 kilograms. The Ô¨Åsh in the river die.
Biologists determine that k kilograms of pollutant sufÔ¨Åce for the Ô¨Åsh to die. Which company
is the cause of the Ô¨Åsh dying if k = 120, if k = 80, and if k = 50?
It is easy to see that if k = 120, then both companies are causes of the Ô¨Åsh dying, according
to all three deÔ¨Ånitions (each company is a but-for cause of the outcome). If k = 50, then each
company is still a cause according to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. For example, to
see that company B is a cause, we consider the contingency where company A does not dump
any pollutant. Then the Ô¨Åsh die if company B pollutes, but they survive if B does not pollute.
With the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition, neither company individually is a cause; there is no variable that
we can hold at its actual value that would make company A or company B a but-for cause.
However, both companies together are the cause.
The situation gets more interesting if k = 80. Now the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition says that only
A is a cause; if A dumps 100 tons of pollutant, then what B does has no impact. The original
and updated deÔ¨Ånition also agree that A is a cause if k = 80. Whether B is a cause depends
on the possible amounts of pollutant that A can dump. If A can dump only 0 or 100 kilograms
of pollutant, then B is not a cause; no setting of A‚Äôs action can result in B‚Äôs action making a
difference. However, if A can dump some amount between 20 and 79 kilograms, then B is a
cause.
It‚Äôs not clear what the ‚Äúright‚Äù answer should be here if k = 80. The law typically wants to
declare B a contributing cause to the death of the Ô¨Åsh (in addition to A), but should this depend
on the amount of pollutant that A can dump? As we shall see in Section 6.2, thinking in
terms of responsibility and blame helps clarify the issue (see Example 6.2.5). Under minimal
assumptions about how likely various amounts of pollutant are to be dumped, B will get some
degree of blame according to the modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition, even when it is not a cause.

2.4 Transitivity

2.4
Transitivity
Example 2.4.1 Now consider the following modiÔ¨Åcation of Example 2.3.5.
Suppose that Monday‚Äôs doctor is reliable and administers the medicine Ô¨Årst thing
in the morning, so that Billy is fully recovered by Tuesday afternoon. Tuesday‚Äôs
doctor is also reliable and would have treated Billy if Monday‚Äôs doctor had failed
to. . . . And let us add a twist: one dose of medication is harmless, but two doses
are lethal.
Is the fact that Tuesday‚Äôs doctor did not treat Billy the cause of him being alive (and recovered)
on Wednesday morning?
The causal model MB for this story is straightforward. There are three variables:
MT for Monday‚Äôs treatment (1 if Billy was treated Monday; 0 otherwise);
TT for Tuesday‚Äôs treatment (1 if Billy was treated Tuesday; 0 otherwise); and
BMC for Billy‚Äôs medical condition (0 if Billy feels Ô¨Åne both Tuesday morning and
Wednesday morning; 1 if Billy feels sick Tuesday morning, Ô¨Åne Wednesday morning;
2 if Billy feels sick both Tuesday and Wednesday morning; 3 if Billy feels Ô¨Åne Tuesday
morning and is dead Wednesday morning).
We can then describe Billy‚Äôs condition as a function of the four possible combinations of
treatment/nontreatment on Monday and Tuesday. I omit the obvious structural equations cor-
responding to this discussion; the causal network is shown in Figure 2.8.

In the context where Billy is sick and Monday‚Äôs doctor treats him, MT = 1 is a but-for
cause of BMC = 0; if Billy had not been treated on Monday, then Billy would not have felt
Ô¨Åne on Tuesday. MT = 1 is also a but-for cause of TT = 0, and TT = 0 is a but-for cause
of Billy‚Äôs being alive (BMC Ã∏= 3, or equivalently, BMC = 0 ‚à®BMC = 1 ‚à®BMC = 2).
However, MT = 1 is not part of a cause of Billy‚Äôs being alive, according to any variant of the

42
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
HP deÔ¨Ånition. It sufÔ¨Åces to show that it is not a cause according the original HP deÔ¨Ånition.
This follows because setting MT = 0 cannot result in Billy dying, not matter how we Ô¨Åx TT.
This shows that causality is not transitive according to the HP deÔ¨Ånition. Although MT =
1 is a cause of TT = 0 and TT = 0 is a cause of BMC = 0 ‚à®BMC = 1 ‚à®BMC = 2,
MT = 1 is not a cause of BMC = 0 ‚à®BMC = 1 ‚à®BMC = 2. Nor is causality closed
under right weakening; that is, replacing a conclusion by something it implies. If A is a cause
of B and B logically implies B‚Ä≤, then A may not be a cause of B‚Ä≤. In this case, MT = 1 is a
cause of BMC = 0, which logically implies BMC = 0 ‚à®BMC = 1 ‚à®BMC = 2, which is
not caused by MT = 1.
Although this example may seem somewhat forced, there are many quite realistic exam-
ples of lack of transitivity with exactly the same structure. Consider the body‚Äôs homeostatic
system. An increase in external temperature causes a short-term increase in core body tem-
perature, which in turn causes the homeostatic system to kick in and return the body to normal
core body temperature shortly thereafter. But if we say that the increase in external tempera-
ture happened at time 0 and the return to normal core body temperature happened at time 1,
we certainly would not want to say that the increase in external temperature at time 0 caused
the body temperature to be normal at time 1!
There is another reason that causality is intransitive, which is illustrated by the following
example.
Example 2.4.2
Suppose that a dog bites Jim‚Äôs right hand. Jim was planning to detonate a
bomb, which he normally would do by pressing the button with his right foreÔ¨Ånger. Because
of the dog bite, he presses the button with his left foreÔ¨Ånger. The bomb still goes off.
Consider the causal model with variables DB (the dog bites, with values 0 and 1), P (the
press of the button, with values 0, 1, and 2, depending on whether the button is not pressed
at all, pressed with the right hand, or pressed with the left hand), and B (the bomb goes off).
We have the obvious equations: DB is determined by the context, P = DB + 1, and B = 1
if P is either 1 or 2. In the context where DB = 1, it is clear that DB = 1 is a but-for cause
of P = 2 (if the dog had not bitten, P would have been 1), and P = 2 is a but-for cause of
B = 1 (if P were 0, then B woud be 0), but DB = 1 is not a cause of P = 1. Regardless
of whether the dog had bitten Jim, the button would have been pressed, and the bomb would
have detonated.
In retrospect, the failure of right weakening is not so surprising. Taking true to be a tau-
tology, if A is a cause of B, then we do not want to say that A is a cause of true, although B
logically implies true. However, the failure of transitivity is quite surprising. Indeed, despite
Examples 2.4.1 and 2.4.2, it seems natural to think of causality as transitive. People often
think in terms of causal chains: A caused B, B caused C, C caused D, and therefore A
caused D, where transitivity seems natural (although the law does not treat causality as tran-
sitive in long causal chains; see Section 3.4.4). Not surprisingly, there are some deÔ¨Ånitions
of causality that require causality to be transitive; see the notes at the end of the chapter for
details.
Why do we feel that causality is transitive? I believe that this is because, in typical settings,
causality is indeed transitive. I give below two simple sets of conditions that are sufÔ¨Åcient to
guarantee transitivity. Because I expect that these conditions apply in many cases, it may

2.4 Transitivity
43
explain why we naturally generalize to thinking of causality as transitive and are surprised
when it is not.
For the purposes of this section, I restrict attention to but-for causes. This is what the law
has focused on and seems to be the situation that arises most often in practice (although I will
admit I have only anecdotal evidence to support this); certainly the law has done reasonably
well by considering only but-for causality. Restricting to but-for causality has the further
advantage that all the variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition agree on what counts as a cause. (Thus, in
this section, I do not specify which variant of the deÔ¨Ånition I am considering.) Restricting to
but-for causality does not solve the transitivity problem. As Examples 2.4.1 and 2.4.2 already
show, even if X1 = x1 is a but-for cause of X2 = x2 and X2 = x2 is a but-for cause of
X3 = x3, it may not be the case that X1 = x1 is a cause of X3 = x3.
The Ô¨Årst set of conditions assumes that X1, X2, and X3 each has a default setting. Such
default settings make sense in many applications; ‚Äúnothing happens‚Äù can be often taken as the
default. Suppose, for example, a billiards expert hits ball A, causing it to hit ball B, causing
it to carom into ball C, which then drops into the pocket. In this case, we can take the default
setting for the shot to be the expert doing nothing and the default setting for the balls to be
that they are not in motion. Let the default setting be denoted by the value 0.
Proposition 2.4.3 Suppose that
(a) X1 = x1 is a but-for cause of X2 = x2 in (M, ‚Éóu),
(b) X2 = x2 is a but-for cause of X3 = x3 in (M, ‚Éóu),
(c) x3 Ã∏= 0,
(d) (M, ‚Éóu) |= [X1 ‚Üê0](X2 = 0), and
(e) (M, ‚Éóu) |= [X1 ‚Üê0, X2 ‚Üê0](X3 = 0).
Then X1 = x1 is a but-for cause of X3 = x3 in (M, ‚Éóu).
Proof:
If X2 = 0 in the unique solution to the equations in the causal model MX1‚Üê0 in
context ‚Éóu and X3 = 0 in the unique solution to the equations in MX1‚Üê0,X2‚Üê0 in context ‚Éóu,
then it is immediate that X3 = 0 in the unique solution to the equations in MX1‚Üê0 in context
‚Éóu. That is, (M, ‚Éóu) |= [X1 ‚Üê0](X3 = 0). It follows from assumption (a) that (M, ‚Éóu) |=
X1 = x1. We must thus have x1 Ã∏= 0; otherwise, (M, ‚Éóu) |= X1 = 0 ‚àß[X1 ‚Üê0](X3 = 0),
so (M, ‚Éóu) |= X3 = 0, which contradicts assumptions (b) and (c). Thus, X1 = x1 is a but-for
cause of X3 = x3, since the value of X3 depends counterfactually on that of X1.
Although the conditions of Proposition 2.4.3 are clearly rather specialized, they arise often
in practice. Conditions (d) and (e) say that if X1 remains in its default state, then so will
X2, and if both X1 and X2 remain in their default states, then so will X3. Put another way,
this says that the reason for X2 not being in its default state is X1 not being in its default
state, and the reason for X3 not being in its default state is X1 and X2 both not being in their
default states. The billiard example can be viewed as a paradigmatic example of when these
conditions apply. It seems reasonable to assume that if the expert does not shoot, then ball A

44
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
does not move; and if the expert does not shoot and ball A does not move (in the context of
interest), then ball B does not move, and so on.
Of course, the conditions on Proposition 2.4.3 do not apply in either Example 2.4.1 or
Example 2.4.2. The obvious default values in Example 2.4.1 are MT = TT = 0, but
the equations say that in all contexts ‚Éóu of the causal model MB for this example, we have
(MB, ‚Éóu) |= [MT ‚Üê0](TT = 1). In the second example, if we take DB = 0 and P = 0 to
be the default values of DB and P, then in all contexts ‚Éóu of the causal model MD, we have
(MD, ‚Éóu) |= [DB ‚Üê0](P = 1).
While Proposition 2.4.3 is useful, there are many examples where there is no obvious
default value. When considering the body‚Äôs homeostatic system, even if there is arguably a
default value for core body temperature, what is the default value for the external temperature?
But it turns out that the key ideas of the proof of Proposition 2.4.3 apply even if there is
no default value. Suppose that X1 = x1 is a but-for cause of X2 = x2 in (M, ‚Éóu) and
X2 = x2 is a but-for cause of X3 = x3 in (M, ‚Éóu). Then to get transitivity, it sufÔ¨Åces
to Ô¨Ånd values x‚Ä≤

To see how these ideas apply, suppose that a student receives an A+ in a course, which
causes her to be accepted at Cornell University (her top choice, of course!), which in turn
causes her to move to Ithaca. Further suppose that if she had received an A in the course,
she would have gone to university U1 and as a result moved to city C1, and if she had gotten
anything else, she would have gone to university at U2 and moved to city C2. This story can
be captured by a causal model with three variables: G for her grade, U for the university she
goes to, and C for the city she moves to. There are no obvious default values for any of these
three variables. Nevertheless, we have transitivity here: The student‚Äôs A+ was a cause of her
being accepted at Cornell, and being accepted at Cornell was a cause of her move to Ithaca;
it seems like a reasonable conclusion that the student‚Äôs A+ was a cause of her move to Ithaca.
Indeed, transitivity follows from Proposition 2.4.4. We can take the student getting an A to be

In light of Propositions 2.4.4 and 2.4.5, understanding why causality is so often taken
to be transitive comes down to Ô¨Ånding sufÔ¨Åcient conditions to guarantee the assumptions of
Proposition 2.4.4. I now present a set of conditions sufÔ¨Åcient to guarantee the assumptions
of Proposition 2.4.4, motivated by the two examples showing that causality is not transitive.
To deal with the problem in Example 2.4.2, I require that for every value x‚Ä≤

cause of X2 = x2 and X2 is binary (since but-for causality requires that two different values
of X1 result in different values of X2). But this requirement does not hold in Example 2.4.2;
no setting of DB can force P to be 0.
Imposing this requirement still does not deal with the problem in Example 2.4.1. To do
that, we need one more condition: X2 must lie on every causal path from X1 to X3. Roughly
speaking, this says that all the inÔ¨Çuence of X1 on X3 goes through X2. This condition does
not hold in Example 2.4.1: as Figure 2.8 shows, there is a direct causal path from MT to
BMC that does not include TT. However, this condition does hold in many cases of interest.
Going back to the example of the student‚Äôs grade, the only way that the student‚Äôs grade can
inÔ¨Çuence which city the student moves to is via the university that accepts the student.
To make this precise, I Ô¨Årst need to deÔ¨Åne causal path. A causal path in a causal setting
(M, ‚Éóu) is a sequence (Y1, . . . , Yk) of variables such that Yj+1 depends on Yj in context ‚Éóu for
j = 1, . . . , k ‚àí1. Since there is an edge between Yj and Yj+1 in the causal network for M
(assuming that we Ô¨Åx context ‚Éóu) exactly if Yj+1 depends on Yj, a causal path is just a path in
the causal network. A causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from X1 to X2 is just a causal path whose Ô¨Årst
node is X1 and whose last node is X2. Finally, Y lies on a causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from X1 to
X2 if Y is a node (possibly X1 or X2) on a causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from X1 to X2.
The following result summarizes the second set of conditions sufÔ¨Åcient for transitivity.
(Recall that R(X) denotes the range of the variable X.)
Proposition 2.4.6 Suppose that X1 = x1 is a but-for cause of X2 = x2 in the causal setting
(M, ‚Éóu), X2 = x2 is a but-for cause of X3 = x3 in (M, ‚Éóu), and the following two conditions
hold:
(a) for every value x‚Ä≤

1 ‚ààR(X1) such that (M, ‚Éóu) |=
[X1 ‚Üêx‚Ä≤

2) (i.e., (X2)x‚Ä≤

2);

Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
(b) X2 is on every causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from X1 to X3.
Then X1 = x1 is a but-for cause of X3 = x3.
The proof of Proposition 2.4.6 is not hard, although we must be careful to get all the details
right. The high-level idea of the proof is easy to explain. Suppose that X2 = x2 is a but-for
cause of X3 = x3 in (M, ‚Éóu). Then there must be some values x2 Ã∏= x‚Ä≤
2 and x3 Ã∏= x‚Ä≤
3 such
that (M, ‚Éóu) |= [X2 ‚Üêx‚Ä≤
2](X3 = x‚Ä≤
3). By assumption, there exists a value x‚Ä≤
1 ‚ààR(X1) such
that (M, ‚Éóu) |= [X1 ‚Üêx‚Ä≤
1](X2 = x‚Ä≤
2). The requirement that X2 is on every causal path from
X1 to X3 guarantees that [X2 ‚Üêx‚Ä≤
2](X3 = x3) implies [X1 ‚Üêx‚Ä≤
1, X2 ‚Üêx‚Ä≤
2](X3 = x3) in
(M, ‚Éóu) (i.e., (X3)x‚Ä≤
