off‚Äù the effect of X1 on X3, since it is on every causal path from X1 to X3. Now we can
apply Proposition 2.4.4. I defer the formal argument to Section 2.10.2.
It is easy to construct examples showing that the conditions of Proposition 2.4.6 are not
necessary for causality to be transitive. Suppose that X1 = x1 causes X2 = x2, X2 = x2
causes X3 = x3, and there are several causal paths from X1 to X3. Roughly speaking, the
reason that X1 = x1 may not be a but-for cause of X3 = x3 is that the effects of X1 on X3
may ‚Äúcancel out‚Äù along the various causal paths. This is what happens in the homeostasis
example. If X2 is on all the causal paths from X1 to X3, then as we have seen, all the effect
of X1 on X3 is mediated by X2, so the effect of X1 on X3 on different causal paths cannot
‚Äúcancel out‚Äù. But even if X2 is not on all the causal paths from X1 to X3, the effects of X1 on
X3 may not cancel out along the causal paths, and X1 = x1 may still be a cause of X3 = x3.
That said, it seems difÔ¨Åcult to Ô¨Ånd a weakening of the condition in Proposition 2.4.6 that is
simple to state and sufÔ¨Åces for causality to be transitive.
2.5
Probability and Causality
In general, an agent trying to determine whether A is a cause of B may not know the exact
model or context. An agent may be uncertain about whether Suzy and Billy both threw, or
just Suzy threw; he may be uncertain about who will hit Ô¨Årst if both Suzy and Billy throw
(or perhaps they hit simultaneously). In a perhaps more interesting setting, at some point,
people were uncertain about whether smoking caused cancer or whether smoking and cancer
were both the outcome of the same genetic problem, which would lead to smoking and cancer
being correlated, but no causal relationship between the two. To deal with such uncertainty,
an agent can put a probability on causal settings. For each causal setting (M, ‚Éóu), the agent
can determine whether A is a cause of B in (M, ‚Éóu), and so compute the probability that A
is a cause of B. Having probabilities on causal settings will be relevant in the discussion of
blame in Chapter 6. Here I focus on what seems to be a different source of uncertainty: un-
certainty in the equations. In causal models, all the equations are assumed to be deterministic.
However, as was observed, in many cases, it seems more reasonable to think of outcomes
as probabilistic. So rather than thinking of Suzy‚Äôs rock as deÔ¨Ånitely hitting the bottle if she
throws, we can think of her as hitting with probability .9. That is, rather than taking Suzy to
always be accurate, we can take her to be accurate with some probability.
The Ô¨Årst step in considering how to deÔ¨Åne causality in the presence of uncertain outcomes
is to show how this uncertainty can be captured in the formal framework. Earlier, I assumed

2.5 Probability and Causality
47
that, for each endogenous variable X, there was a deterministic function FX that described
the value of X as a function of all the other variables. Now, rather than assuming that FX
returns a particular value, I assume that it returns a distribution over the values of X given the
values of all other variables. This is perhaps easiest to understand by means of an example.
In the rock-throwing example, rather than assuming that if Suzy throws a rock, she deÔ¨Ånitely
hits the bottle, suppose we assume that she only only hits it with probability .9 and misses
with probability .1. Suppose that if Billy throws and Suzy hasn‚Äôt hit the bottle, then Billy will
hit it with probability .8 and miss with probability .2. Finally, for simplicity, assume that, if
hit by either Billy or Suzy, the bottle will deÔ¨Ånitely shatter.
The next two paragraphs show how the probabilistic version of this story can be modeled
formally in the structural-equations framework and can be skipped on a Ô¨Årst reading. Recall
that for the more sophisticated model M ‚Ä≤
RT of the (deterministic) rock-throwing story, al-
though I earlier wrote the equation for SH as SH = ST, this is really an abbreviation for the
function FSH (i1, i2, i3, i4, i5) = i2: FSH is a function of the values of the exogenous variable
U (which is not described in the causal network but determines the values of BT and ST) and
the endogenous variables ST, BT, BH , and BS. These values are given by i1, . . . , i5. The
equation SH = ST says that the value of SH depends only on the value of ST and is equal to
it; that explains the output i2. For the probabilistic version of the story, FSH (i1, 1, i3, i4, i5)
is the probability distribution that places probability .9 on the value 1 and probability .1 on
the value 0. In words, this says that if Suzy throws (ST = 1), then no matter what the values
i1, i3, i4, and i5 of U, BT, BH , and BS are, respectively, FSH (i1, 1, i3, i4, i5) is the proba-
bility distribution that puts probability .9 on the event SH = 1 and probability .1 on the event
SH = 0. This captures the fact that if Suzy throws, then she has a probability .9 of hitting
the bottle (no matter what Billy does). Similarly, FSH (i1, 0, i3, i4, i5) is the distribution that
places probability 1 on the value 0: if Suzy doesn‚Äôt throw, then she certainly won‚Äôt hit the
bottle, so the event SH = 0 has probability 1.
Similarly, in the deterministic model of the rock-throwing example, The function FBH is
such that FBH (i1, i2, i3, i4, i5) = 1 if (i3, i4) = (1, 0), and is 0 otherwise. That is, taking
i1, . . . , i5 to be the values of U, ST, BT, SH , and BS, respectively, Billy‚Äôs throw hits the
bottle only if he throws (i3 = 1) and Suzy doesn‚Äôt hit the bottle (i4 = 0). For the probabilistic
version of the story, FBH (i1, i2, i3, i4, i5) is the distribution that puts probability .8 on 1 and
probability .2 on 0; for all other values of i3 and i4, FBH (i1, i2, 1, 0, i5) puts probability
1 on 0. (Henceforth, I just describe the probabilities in words rather than using the formal
notation.)
The interpretation of these probabilities is similar to the interpretation of the deterministic
equations we have used up to now. For example, the fact that Suzy‚Äôs rock hits with probability
.9 does not mean that the probability of Suzy‚Äôs rock hitting conditional on her throwing is .9;
rather, it means that if there is an intervention that results in Suzy throwing, the probability
of her hitting is .9. The probability of rain conditional on a low barometer reading is high.
However, intervening on the barometer reading, say, by setting the needle to point to a low
reading, does not affect the probability of rain.
There have been many attempts to give a deÔ¨Ånition of causality that takes probability into
account. They typically take A to be a cause of B if A raises the probability of B. I am going
to take a different approach here. I take a standard technique in computer science to ‚Äúpull

48
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
out the probability‚Äù, allowing me to convert a single causal setting where the equations are
probabilistic to a probability over causal settings, where in each causal setting, the equations
are deterministic. This, in turn, will allow me to avoid giving a separate deÔ¨Ånition of prob-
abilistic causality. Rather, I will be able to use the deÔ¨Ånition of causality already given for
deterministic models and talk about the probability of causality, that is, the probability that
A is a cause of B. As we shall see, this approach seems to deal naturally with a number of
problems regarding probabilistic causality that have been raised in the literature.
The assumption is that the equations would be deterministic if we knew all the relevant
details. This assumption is probably false at the quantum level; most physicists believe that
at this level, the world is genuinely probabilistic. But for the macroscopic events that I focus
on in this book (and that most people are interested in when applying causality judgments),
it seems reasonable to view the world as fundamentally deterministic. With this viewpoint, if
Suzy hits the rock with probability .9, then there must be (perhaps poorly understood) reasons
that cause her to miss: an unexpected gust of wind or a momentary distraction throwing off
her aim. We can ‚Äúpackage up‚Äù all these factors and make them exogenous. That is, we can
have an exogenous variable U ‚Ä≤ with values either 0 or 1 depending on whether the features
that cause Suzy to miss are present, where U ‚Ä≤ = 0 with probability .1 and U ‚Ä≤ = 1 with
probability .9. By introducing such a variable U ‚Ä≤ (and a corresponding variable for Billy),
we have essentially pulled the probability out of the equations and put it into the exogenous
variable.
I now show in more detail how this approach plays out in the context of the rock-throwing
example with the probabilities given above. Consider the causal setting where Suzy and Billy
both throw. We may be interested in knowing the probability that Suzy or Billy will be a cause
of the bottle shattering if we don‚Äôt know whether the bottle will in fact shatter, or in knowing
the probability that Suzy or Billy is the cause if we know that the bottle actually did shatter.
(Notice that in the former case, we are asking about an effect of a (possible) cause, whereas in
the latter case, we are asking about the cause of an effect.) When we pull out the probability,
there are four causal models that arise here, depending on whether Suzy‚Äôs rock hits the bottle
and whether Billy‚Äôs rock hits the bottle if Suzy‚Äôs rock does not hit. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, consider the
following four models, in a context where both Suzy and Billy throw rocks:
M1: Suzy‚Äôs rock hits and Billy‚Äôs rock would hit if Suzy missed;
M2: Suzy‚Äôs rock hits but Billy‚Äôs rock would not hit if Suzy missed;
M3: Suzy‚Äôs rock misses and Billy‚Äôs rock hits;
M4: neither rock hits.
Note that these models differ in their structural equations, although they involve the same
exogenous and endogenous variables. M1 is just the model considered earlier, described in
Figure 2.3. Suzy‚Äôs throw is a cause in M1 and M2; Billy‚Äôs throw is a cause in M3.
If we know that the bottle shattered, Suzy‚Äôs rock hit, and Billy‚Äôs didn‚Äôt, then the probability
that Suzy is the cause is 1. We are conditioning on the model being either M1 or M2 because
these are the models where Suzy‚Äôs rock hits, and Suzy is the cause of the bottle shattering in
both. But suppose that all we know is that the bottle shattered, and we are trying to determine

2.5 Probability and Causality
49
the probability that Suzy is the cause. Now we can condition on the model being either M1,
M2, or M3, so if the prior probability of M4 is p, then the probability that Suzy is a cause of
the bottle shattering is .9/(1 ‚àíp): Suzy is a cause of the bottle shattering as long as she hits
the bottle and it shatters (i.e., in models M1 and M2). Unfortunately, the story does not give
us that the probability of M4. It is .1 ‚àíq, where q is the prior probability of M3, but the story
does not tell us that either. We can say more if we make a reasonable additional assumption:
that whether Suzy‚Äôs rock hits is independent of whether Billy‚Äôs rock would hit if Suzy missed.
Then M1 has probability .72 (= .9 √ó .8), M2 has probability .18, M3 has probability .08, and
M4 has probability .02. With this assumption and no further information, the probability of
Suzy being a cause of the bottle shatter is .9/.98 = 45/49.
Although it seems reasonable to view the outcomes ‚ÄúSuzy‚Äôs rock hits the bottle‚Äù and
‚ÄúBilly‚Äôs rock would have hit the bottle if Suzy‚Äôs hadn‚Äôt‚Äù as independent, they certainly do
not have to be. We could, for example, assume that Billy and Suzy are perfectly accurate
when there are no wind gusts and miss when there are wind gusts, and that wind gusts occur
with probability .7. Then it would be the case that Suzy‚Äôs rock hits with probability .7 and
that Billy‚Äôs rock would have hit with probability .7 if Suzy‚Äôs had missed, but there would have
been only two causal models: the one that we considered in the deterministic case, which
would be the actual model with probability .7, and the one where both miss. (A better model
of this situation might have an exogenous variable U ‚Ä≤ such that U ‚Ä≤ = 1 if there are wind gusts
and U ‚Ä≤ = 0 if there aren‚Äôt, and then have deterministic equations for SH and BH .) The key
message here is that, in general, further information might be needed to determine the prob-
ability of causality beyond what is provided by probabilistic structural equations as deÔ¨Åned
above.
A few more examples should help illustrate how this approach works and how much we
can say.
Example 2.5.1
Suppose that a doctor treats Billy on Monday. The treatment will result in
Billy recovering on Tuesday with probability .9. But Billy might also recover with no treat-
ment due to some other factors, with independent probability .1. By ‚Äúindependent probability‚Äù
here, I mean that conditional on Billy being treated on Monday, the events ‚ÄúBilly recovers due
to the treatment‚Äù and ‚ÄúBilly recovers due to other factors‚Äù are independent; thus, the probabil-
ity that Billy will not recover on Tuesday given that he is treated on Monday is (.1)(.9). Now,
in fact, Billy is treated on Monday and he recovers on Tuesday. Is the treatment the cause of
Billy getting better?
The standard answer is yes, because the treatment greatly increases the likelihood of Billy
getting better. But there is still the nagging concern that Billy‚Äôs recovery was not due to the
treatment. He might have gotten better if he had just been left alone.
Formally, we have a causal model with three endogenous binary variables: MT (the doctor
treats Billy on Monday), OF (the other factors that would make Billy get better occur), and
BMC (Billy‚Äôs medical condition, which we can take to be 1 if he gets better and 0 otherwise).
The exogenous variable(s) determine MT and OF. The probability that MT = 1 does not
matter for this analysis; for deÔ¨Åniteness, assume that MT = 1 with probability .8. According
to the story, OF = 1 with probability .9. Finally, BMC = 1 if OF = 1, BMC = 0 if
MT = OF = 0, and BMC = 1 with probability .9 if MT = 1 and OF = 0. Again, after
we pull out the probability, there are eight models, which differ in whether MT is set to 0 or

50
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
1, whether OF is set to 0 or 1, and whether BMC = 1 when MT = 1 and OF = 0. Treating
these choices as independent (as is suggested by the story), it is straightforward to calculate
the probability of each of the eight models. Since we know that Billy actually did get better
and was treated by his doctor, we ignore the four models where MT = 0 and the model where
MT = 1, OF = 0, and BMC = 0 if MT = 1 and OF = 0. The remaining three models
have probability .8(1‚àí(.9)(.1)). MT = 1 is a cause of BMC = 0 in two of the three models;
it is not a cause only in the model where OF = 1, MT = 1, but BMC would be 0 if OF
were set to 0. This latter model has prior probability .8(.1)(.1). Straightforward calculations
now show that the treatment cause of Billy‚Äôs recovery with probability
.9
1‚àí(.9)(.1) = 90/91.
Similarly, the probability that Billy‚Äôs recovery was caused by other factors is
.1
1‚àí(.1)(.9) =
10/91. These probabilities sum to more than 1 because with probability
(.1)(.9)
1‚àí(.1)(.9) = 9/91,
both are causes; Billy‚Äôs recovery is overdetermined.
Now suppose that we can perform a (somewhat expensive) test to determine whether the
doctor‚Äôs treatment actually caused Billy to get better. Again, the doctor treats Billy and Billy
recovers, but the test is carried out, and it is shown that the treatment was not the cause. It is
still the case that the treatment signiÔ¨Åcantly raises the probability of Billy recovering, but now
we certainly don‚Äôt want to call the treatment the cause. And, indeed, it is not. Conditioning
on the information, we can discard three of the four models. The only one left is the one in
which Billy‚Äôs recovery is due to other factors (with probability 1). Moreover, this conclusion
does not depend on the independence assumption.
Example 2.5.2
Now suppose that there is another doctor who can treat Billy on Monday,
with different medication. Both treatments are individually effective with probability .9. Un-
fortunately, if Billy gets both treatments and both are effective, then, with probability .8, there
will be a bad interaction, and Billy will die. For this version of the story, suppose that there are
no other factors involved; if Billy gets neither treatment, he will not recover. Further suppose
that we can perform a test to see whether a treatment was effective. Suppose that in fact both
doctors treat Billy and, despite that, Billy recovers on Tuesday. Without further information,
there are three relevant models: in the Ô¨Årst, only the Ô¨Årst doctor‚Äôs medication was effective; in
the second, only the second doctor‚Äôs medication was effective; in the third, both are effective.
If further testing shows that both treatments were effective, then there is no uncertainty; we
are down to just one model: the third one. According to the original and updated HP def-
inition, both treatments are the cause of Billy‚Äôs recovery, with probability 1. (According to
the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, they are both parts of the cause.) This is true despite the fact that,
given that the Ô¨Årst doctor treated Billy, the second doctor treating Billy signiÔ¨Åcantly lowers the
probability of Billy recovering (and symmetrically with the roles of the doctors reversed).
Example 2.5.3 Now suppose that a doctor has treated 1,000 patients. They each would have
had probability .9 of recovering even without the treatment. With the treatment, they recover
with independent probability .1 In fact, 908 patients recovered, and Billy is one of them. What
is the probability that the treatment was the cause of Billy‚Äôs recovery? Calculations similar
to those in Example 2.5.1 show that it is 10/91. Standard probabilistic calculations show that
there is quite a high probability that the treatment is a cause of at least one patient‚Äôs recovery.
Indeed, there is quite a high probability that the treatment is the unique cause of at least one
patient‚Äôs recovery. But we do not know which patient that is.

2.5 Probability and Causality
51
Example 2.5.4 Again, Billy and Suzy throw rocks at a bottle. If either Billy or Suzy throws a
rock, it will hit the bottle. But now the bottle is heavy and will, in general, not topple over if it
is hit. If only Billy hits the bottle, it will topple over with probability .2; similarly, if only Suzy
hits the bottle, it will topple over with probability .2. If they hit the bottle simultaneously, it
will topple with probability .7. In fact, both Billy and Suzy throw rocks at the bottle, and it
topples over. We are interested in the extent to which Suzy and Billy are the causes of the
bottle toppling over.
For simplicity, I restrict attention to contexts where both Billy and Suzy throw, both hit,
and the bottle topples over. After pulling out the probability, there are four causal models of
interest, depending on what would have happened if just Suzy had hit the bottle and if just
Billy had hit the bottle:
M1: the bottle would have toppled over if either only Suzy‚Äôs rock had hit it or if only
Billy‚Äôs rock had hit it;
M2: the bottle would have toppled over if only Suzy‚Äôs rock had hit it but not if only
Billy‚Äôs rock had hit it;
M3: the bottle would have toppled over if only Billy‚Äôs rock had hit it but not if only
Suzy‚Äôs rock had hit it;
M4: the bottle would not have toppled over if either only Billy‚Äôs rock or only Suzy‚Äôs
rock had hit it.
I further simplify by treating the outcomes ‚Äúthe bottle would have toppled over had only
Suzy‚Äôs rock hit it‚Äù and ‚Äúthe bottle would have toppled over had only Billy‚Äôs rock hit it‚Äù as
independent (even though neither may be independent of the outcome ‚Äúthe bottle would have
toppled over had both Billy and Suzy‚Äôs rocks hit it‚Äù). With this assumption, the probability
of M1 (conditional on the bottle toppling over if both Suzy‚Äôs and Billy‚Äôs rock hit it) is .04,
the conditional probability of M2 is .16, the conditional probability of M3 is .16, and the
conditional probability of M4 is .64.
It is easy to check that Suzy‚Äôs throw is a cause of the bottle toppling in models M1, M2, and
M4, but not in M3. Similarly, Billy‚Äôs throw is a cause in models M1, M3, and M4. They are
both causes in models M1 and M4 according to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, as well
as in M4 according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition; in M1, the conjunction ST = 1 ‚àßBT = 1
is a cause according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. Thus, the probability that Suzy‚Äôs throw is
part of a cause of the bottle shattering is .84, and likewise for Billy. The probability that both
are parts of causes is .68 (since this is the case in models M1 and M4).
As these examples show, the HP deÔ¨Ånition lets us make perfectly sensible statements about
the probability of causality. There is nothing special about these examples; all other examples
can be handled equally well. Perhaps the key message here is that there is no need to work
hard on getting a deÔ¨Ånition of probabilistic causality, at least at the macroscopic level; it
sufÔ¨Åces to get a good deÔ¨Ånition of deterministic causality. But what about if we want to treat
events at the quantum level, which seems inherently probabilistic? A case can still be made
that it is psychologically useful to think deterministically. Whether the HP deÔ¨Ånitions can be
extended successfully to an inherently probabilistic framework still remains open.

52
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
Even if we ignore issues at the microscopic level, there is still an important question to be
addressed: In what sense is the single model with probabilistic equations equivalent to the
set of deterministic causal models with a probability on them? In a naive sense, the answer
is no. The examples above show that the probabilistic equations do not in general sufÔ¨Åce to
determine the probability of the deterministic causal models. Thus, there is a sense in which
deterministic causal models with a probability on them carry more information than a single
probabilistic model. Moreover, this extra information is useful. As we have seen, it may be
necessary to determine the answer to questions such as ‚ÄúHow probable is it that Billy is the
cause of the bottle shattering?‚Äù when all we see is a shattered bottle and know that Billy and
Suzy both threw. Such a determination can be quite important in, for example, legal cases.
Although bottle shattering may not be of great legal interest, we may well want to know the
probability of Bob being the cause of Charlie dying in a case where there are multiple potential
causes.
If we make reasonable independence assumptions, then a probabilistic model determines a
probability on deterministic models. Moreover, under the obvious assumptions regarding the
semantics of causal models with probabilistic equations, formulas in the language described
in Section 2.2.1 have the same probability of being true under both approaches. Consider
Example 2.5.4 again. What is the probability of a formula like [ST ‚Üê0](BS = 1) (if Suzy
hadn‚Äôt thrown, the bottle would have shattered)? Clearly, of the four deterministic models
described in the discussion of this example, the formula is true only in M1 and M3; thus, it
has probability .2. Although I have not given a semantics for formulas in this language in
probabilistic causal models (i.e., models where the equations are probabilistic), if we assume
that the outcomes ‚Äúthe bottle would have toppled over had only Suzy‚Äôs rock hit it‚Äù and ‚Äúthe
bottle would have toppled over had only Billy‚Äôs rock hit it‚Äù are independent, as I assumed
when assigning probabilities to the deterministic models, we would expect this statement to
also be true in the probabilistic model. The converse holds as well: if a statement about the
probability of causality holds in the probabilistic model, then it holds in the corresponding
deterministic model.
This means that (under the independence assumption) as far as the causal language of Sec-
tion 2.2.1 is concerned, the two approaches are equivalent. Put another way, to distinguish the
two approaches, we need a richer language. More generally, although we can debate whether
pulling out the probability ‚Äúreally‚Äù gives an equivalent formulation of the situation, to the ex-
tent that the notion of (probabilistic) causality is expressible in the language of Section 2.2.1,
the two approaches are equivalent. In this sense, it is safe to reduce to deterministic models.
This observation leads to two further points. Suppose that we do not want to make the
relevant independence assumptions. Is there a natural way to augment probabilistic causal
models to represent this information (beyond just going directly to a probability over deter-
ministic causal models)? See Section 5.1 for further discussion of this point.
Of course, we do not have to use probability to represent uncertainty; other representations
of uncertainty could be used as well. Indeed, there is a beneÔ¨Åt to using a representation
that involves sets of probabilities. Since, in general, probabilistic structural equations do
not determine the probability of the deterministic models that arise when we pull out the
probability, all we have is a set of possible probabilities on the deterministic models. Given
that, it seems reasonable to start with sets of probabilities on the equations as well. This

2.6 SufÔ¨Åcient Causality
53
makes sense if, for example, we do not know the exact probability that Suzy‚Äôs rock would hit
the bottle, but we know that it would hit it with probability between .6 and .8. It seems that
the same general approach of pulling out the probabilities works if we represent uncertainty
using sets of probabilities, but I have not explored this approach in detail.
2.6
SufÔ¨Åcient Causality
Although counterfactual dependence is a key feature of causality, people‚Äôs causality judg-
ments are clearly inÔ¨Çuenced by another quite different feature: how sensitive the causality
ascription is to changes in various other factors. If we assume that Suzy is very accurate, then
her throwing a rock is a robust cause of the bottle shattering. The bottle will shatter regardless
of whether Billy throws, even if the bottle is in a slightly different position and even if it is
slightly windy. On the other hand, suppose instead that we consider a long causal chain like
the following: Suzy throws a rock at a lock, causing it to open, causing the lion that was in
the locked cage to escape, frightening the cat, which leapt up on to the table and knocked over
the bottle, which then shattered. Although Suzy‚Äôs throw is still a but-for cause of the bottle
shattering, the shattering is clearly sensitive to many other factors. If Suzy‚Äôs throw had not
broken the lock, if the lion had run in a different direction, or if the cat had not jumped on
the table, then the bottle would not have shattered. People seem inclined to assign less blame
to Suzy‚Äôs throw in this case. I return to the issue of long causal chains in Sections 3.4.4 and
provide a solution to it using the notion of blame in Section 6.2. Here I deÔ¨Åne a notion of suf-
Ô¨Åcient causality that captures some of the intuitions behind insensitive causation. SufÔ¨Åcient
causality also turns out to be related to the notion of explanation that I deÔ¨Åne in Chapter 7, so
it is worth considering in its own right, although it will not be a focus of this book.
The key intuition behind the deÔ¨Ånition of sufÔ¨Åcient causality is that not only does ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx
sufÔ¨Åce to bring about œï in the actual context (which is the intuition that AC2(bo) and AC2(bu)
are trying to capture), but it also brings it about in other ‚Äúnearby‚Äù contexts. Since the frame-
work does not provide a metric on contexts, there is no obvious way to deÔ¨Åne nearby context.
Thus, in the formal deÔ¨Ånition below, I start by considering all contexts. Since conjunction
plays a somewhat different role in this deÔ¨Ånition than it does in the deÔ¨Ånition of actual causal-
ity (particularly in the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition), I take seriously here the intuition that what we
really care about are parts of causes, rather than causes.
Again, there are three variants of the notion of sufÔ¨Åcient causality, depending on which
version of AC2 we use. The differences between these variants do not play a role in our
discussion, so I just write AC2 below. Of course, if we want to distinguish, AC2 below can
be replaced by either AC2(a) and AC2(bo), AC2(a) and AC2(bu), or AC2(am), depending on
which version of the HP deÔ¨Ånition is being considered.
DeÔ¨Ånition 2.6.1
‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a sufÔ¨Åcient cause of œï in the causal setting (M, ‚Éóu) if the following
conditions hold:
SC1. (M, ‚Éóu) |= ( ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx) and (M, ‚Éóu) |= œï.
SC2. Some conjunct of ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is part of a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu). More precisely, there exists
a conjunct X = x of ‚ÉóX = ‚ÉóX and another (possibly empty) conjunction ‚ÉóY = ‚Éóy such

54
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
that X = x ‚àß‚ÉóY = ‚Éóy is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu); that is, AC1, AC2, and AC3 hold for
X = x ‚àß‚ÉóY = ‚Éóy.
SC3. (M, ‚Éóu‚Ä≤) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx]œï for all contexts ‚Éóu‚Ä≤.
SC4. ‚ÉóX is minimal; there is no strict subset ‚ÉóX‚Ä≤ of ‚ÉóX such that ‚ÉóX‚Ä≤ = ‚Éóx‚Ä≤ satisÔ¨Åes conditions
SC1, SC2, and SC3, where ‚Éóx‚Ä≤ is the restriction of ‚Éóx to the variables in ‚ÉóX.
SC3 is the key condition here; it says that ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx sufÔ¨Åces to bring about œï in all contexts.
Thus, in the case of the 11‚Äì0 victory, any set of six voters becomes a sufÔ¨Åcient cause (no mat-
ter which variant of the HP deÔ¨Ånition we use), assuming that there are contexts corresponding
to all possible voting conÔ¨Ågurations. This suggests that sufÔ¨Åcient causality is related to the
modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, which, in this case, would also take any subset of six voters to be a
cause. However, this is misleading, as the next example shows.
Consider the forest-Ô¨Åre example again. In the disjunctive model, each of L = 1 and
MD = 1 is a sufÔ¨Åcient cause; in the conjunctive model, L = 1 ‚àßMD = 1 is a sufÔ¨Åcient
cause, assuming that there is a context where there is no lightning and another where the
arsonist does not drop a match. This is the case for all variants of the HP deÔ¨Ånition. Recall
that this is just the opposite of what the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition does with actual causality; it
would declare L = 1 ‚àßMD = 1 the cause in the disjunctive model and both L = 1 and
MD = 1 individually causes in the conjunctive model.
I wrote SC2 as I did so as to take seriously the notion that all we really care about when
using the modiÔ¨Åed (and updated) deÔ¨Ånition is parts of causes. Thus, in the disjunctive model
for the forest Ô¨Åre example, L = 1 and MD = 1 are both sufÔ¨Åcient causes of the forest
Ô¨Åre in the context (1, 1) where there is both a lightning strike and a dropped match, even
if we use AC2(am) in SC2.
By assumption, SC3 holds: both [L ‚Üê1](FF = 1) and
[MD ‚Üê1](FF = 1) hold in all contexts. Moreover, both L = 1 and MD = 1 are parts
of a cause of FF = 1 (namely, L = 1 ‚àßMD = 1), so SC2 holds.
The forest-Ô¨Åre example shows that sufÔ¨Åcient causality lets us distinguish what can be called
joint causes from independent causes. In the disjunctive forest-Ô¨Åre model, the lightning and
the dropped match can each be viewed as independent causes of the Ô¨Åre; each sufÔ¨Åces to
bring it about. In the conjunctive model, the lightning and the dropped match are joint causes;
their joint action is needed to bring about the forest Ô¨Åre. The distinction between joint and
independent causality seems to be one that people are quite sensitive to. Not surprisingly, it
plays a role in legal judgments.
Going on with examples, consider the sophisticated rock-throwing model M ‚Ä≤
RT from Ex-
ample 2.3.3 again. Assume that Suzy is always accurate; that is, she is accurate in all contexts
where she throws, and would be accurate even in contexts where she doesn‚Äôt throw if (coun-
terfactually) she were to throw; that is, assume that [ST ‚Üê1](BS = 1) holds in all contexts,
even if Suzy does not actually throw. Then Suzy‚Äôs throw is a sufÔ¨Åcient cause of the bottle
shattering in the context that we have been considering, where both Billy and Suzy throw,
and Suzy‚Äôs throw actually hits the bottle. Billy‚Äôs throw is not a sufÔ¨Åcient cause in this context
because it is not even (part of) a cause. However, if we consider the model M ‚àó
RT, which has a
larger set of contexts, including ones where Suzy might throw and miss, then ST = 1 is not a
sufÔ¨Åcient cause of BS = 1 in the context u‚àówhere Suzy is accurate and her rock hits before

2.6 SufÔ¨Åcient Causality
55
Billy‚Äôs, but SH = 1 is. Recall that ST = 1 remains a cause of BS = 1 in (M ‚àó
RT, u‚àó). This
shows that (part of) a cause is not necessarily part of a sufÔ¨Åcient cause.
In the double-prevention example (Example 2.3.4), assuming that Suzy is accurate,
SBT = 1 (Suzy bombing the target) is a sufÔ¨Åcient cause of TD = 1 (the target being
destroyed). In contrast, Billy pulling the trigger and shooting down Enemy is not, if there are
contexts where Suzy does not go up, and thus does not destroy the target. More generally, a
but-for cause at the beginning of a long causal chain is unlikely to be a sufÔ¨Åcient cause.
The deÔ¨Ånition of actual causality (DeÔ¨Ånition 2.2.1) focuses on the actual context; the set
of possible contexts plays no role. By way of contrast, the set of contexts plays a major
role in the deÔ¨Ånition of sufÔ¨Åcient causality. The use of SC3 makes sufÔ¨Åcient causality quite
sensitive to the choice of the set of possible contexts. It also may make it an unreasonably
strong requirement in some cases. Do we want to require SC3 to hold even in some extremely
unlikely contexts? A sensible way to weaken SC3 would be to add probability to the picture,
especially if we have a probability on contexts, as in Section 2.5. Rather than requiring SC3 to
hold for all contexts, we could then consider the probability of the set of contexts for which it
holds. That is, we can take ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx to be a sufÔ¨Åcient cause of œï with probability Œ± in (M, ‚Éóu) if
SC1, SC2, and SC4 hold and the set of contexts for which SC3 holds has probability at least Œ±.
Notice that with this change, there is a tradeoff between minimality and probability of
sufÔ¨Åciency. Consider Suzy throwing rocks. Suppose that, although Suzy is quite accurate,
there is a (quite unlikely) context where there are high winds, so Suzy misses the bottle. In
this model, a sufÔ¨Åcient cause for the bottle shattering is ‚ÄúSuzy throws and there are no high
winds‚Äù. Suzy throwing is not by itself sufÔ¨Åcient. But it is sufÔ¨Åcient in all contexts but the one
in which there are high winds. If the context with high winds has probability .1, then Suzy‚Äôs
throw is a sufÔ¨Åcient cause of the bottle shattering with probability .9.
This tradeoff between minimality and probability of sufÔ¨Åciency comes up again in the
context of the deÔ¨Ånition of explanation, considered in Section 7.1, so I do not dwell on it
here. But thinking in terms of probability provides some insight into whether not performing
an action should be treated as a sufÔ¨Åcient cause. Consider Example 2.3.5, where Billy is
hospitalized. Besides Billy‚Äôs doctor, there are other doctors at the hospital who could, in
principle, treat Billy. But they are unlikely to do so. Indeed, they will not even check up on
Billy unless they are told to; they presumably have other priorities.
Now consider a context where in fact no doctor treats Billy. In that case, Billy‚Äôs doctor
not treating Billy on Monday is a sufÔ¨Åcient cause for Billy feeling sick on Tuesday with high
probability because, with high probability, no other doctor would treat him either. However,
another doctor not treating Billy has only a low probability of being a sufÔ¨Åcient cause for
Billy feeling sick on Tuesday because, with high probability, Billy‚Äôs doctor does treat him.
This intuition is similar in spirit to the way normality considerations are brought to bear on
this example in Chapter 3 (see Example 3.2.2).
For the most part, I do not focus on sufÔ¨Åcient causality in this book. I think that notions
such as normality, blame, and explanation capture many of the same intuitions as sufÔ¨Åcient
causality in an arguably better way. However, it is worth keeping sufÔ¨Åcient causality in mind
when we consider these other notions.

56
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
2.7
Causality in Nonrecursive Models
Up to now, I have considered causality only in recursive (i.e., acyclic) causal models. Recur-
sive models are also my focus throughout the rest of the book. However, there are examples
that arguably involve nonrecursive models. For example, the connection between pressure
and volume is given by Boyle‚Äôs Law, which says that PV = k: the product of pressure and
volume is a constant (if the temperature remains constant). Thus, increasing the pressure de-
creases the volume, and decreasing the volume increases the pressure. There is no direction
of causality in this equation. However, in any particular setting, we are typically manipulating
either the pressure or the volume, so there is (in that setting) a ‚Äúdirection‚Äù of causality.
Perhaps a more interesting example is one of collusion. Imagine two arsonists who agree
to burn down the forest, when only one match sufÔ¨Åces to burn down the forest. (So we are
essentially in the disjunctive model.) However, the arsonists make it clear to each other that
each will not drop a lighted match unless the other does. At the critical moment, they look
in each others‚Äô eyes, and both drop their lit matches, resulting in the forest burning down. In
this setting, it is plausible that each arsonist caused the other to throw the match, and together
they caused the forest to burn down. That is, if MDi stands for ‚Äúarsonist i drops a lit match‚Äù,
for i = 1, 2, and U is an exogenous variable that determines whether the arsonists initially
intend to drop the match, we can take the equation for MD1 to be such that MD1 = 1 if and
only if U = 1 and MD2 = 1, and similarly for MD2. This model is not recursive; in all
contexts, MD1 depends on MD2 and MD2 depends on MD1. In this section, I show how the
HP deÔ¨Ånition(s) can be extended to deal with such nonrecursive models.
In nonrecursive models, there may be more than one solution to an equation in a given
context, or there may be none. In particular, this means that a context no longer necessarily
determines the values of the endogenous variables. Earlier, I identiÔ¨Åed a primitive event
such as X = x with the basic causal formula [ ](X = x), that is, a formula of the form
[Y1 ‚Üêy1, . . . , Yk ‚Üêyk](X = x) with k = 0. In recursive causal models, where there is
a unique solution to the equations in a given context, we can deÔ¨Åne [ ](X = x) to be true in
(M, ‚Éóu) if X = x is the unique solution to the equations in context ‚Éóu. It seems reasonable to
identify [ ](X = x) with X = x in this case. But it is not so reasonable if there may be several
solutions to the equations or none.
What we really want to do is to be able to say that X = x under a particular setting of the
variables. Thus, we now take the truth of a primitive event such as X = x to be relative not
just to a context but to a complete assignment: a complete description (‚Éóu,‚Éóv) of the values of
both the exogenous and the endogenous variables. As before, the context ‚Éóu assigns a value
to all the exogenous variables; ‚Éóv assigns a value to all the endogenous variables. Now deÔ¨Åne
(M, ‚Éóu,‚Éóv) |= X = x if X has value x in ‚Éóv. Since the truth of X = x depends on just ‚Éóv, not ‚Éóu,
I sometimes write (M,‚Éóv) |= X = x.
This deÔ¨Ånition can be extended to Boolean combinations of primitive events in the standard
way. DeÔ¨Åne (M, ‚Éóu,‚Éóv) |= [‚ÉóY ‚Üê‚Éóy]œï iff (M,‚Éóv‚Ä≤) |= œï for all solutions (‚Éóu,‚Éóv‚Ä≤) to the equations
in M‚ÉóY ‚Üê‚Éóy. Since the truth of [‚ÉóY ‚Üê‚Éóy](X = x) depends only on the setting ‚Éóu of the exogenous
variables, and not on ‚Éóv, I write (M, ‚Éóu) |= [‚ÉóY ‚Üê‚Éóy](X = x) to emphasize this.
With these deÔ¨Ånitions in hand, it is easy to extend the HP deÔ¨Ånition of causality to arbitrary
models. Causality is now deÔ¨Åned with respect to a tuple (M, ‚Éóu,‚Éóv) because we need to know

the values of the endogenous variables to determine the truth of some formulas. That is, we
now talk about ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx being an actual cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu,‚Éóv). As before, we have conditions
AC1‚ÄìAC3. AC1 and AC3 remain unchanged. AC2 depends on whether we want to consider
the original, updated, or modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition. For the updated deÔ¨Ånition, we have:

In recursive models, the formula ¬¨[ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx‚Ä≤, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów‚Ä≤]œï in the version of AC2(a) above is
equivalent to [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx‚Ä≤, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów‚Ä≤]¬¨œï, the formula used in the original formulation of AC2(a).
Given a recursive model M, there is a unique solution to the equations in M ‚Éó

several solutions; AC2(a) holds if œï does not hold in at least one of them. By way of contrast,
for AC2(bu) to hold, œï must stay true in all solutions to the equations in M ‚Éó

is necessary to bring about œï (since setting ‚ÉóX to something other than ‚Éóx in that contingency
may result in œï no longer holding). Requiring ¬¨œï to hold in only one solution to the equations
seems in the spirit of the necessity requirement. However, AC2(bu) says that setting ‚ÉóX to ‚Éóx is
sufÔ¨Åcient to bring about œï in all relevant settings, so it makes sense that we want œï to hold in
all solutions. That‚Äôs part of the intuition for sufÔ¨Åciency. Clearly, in the recursive case, where
there is only one solution, this deÔ¨Ånition agrees with the deÔ¨Ånition given earlier.
Analogous changes are required to extend the original and modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition to the

(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów, ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éóz‚àó]œï, and do not require this to hold for all subsets ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ of
‚ÉóW; for AC2(am), we require that ‚Éów consist of the values of the variables in ‚ÉóW in the actual
context. Again, these deÔ¨Ånitions agree with the earlier deÔ¨Ånitions in the recursive case.
Note that, with these deÔ¨Ånitions, all deÔ¨Ånitions agree that MD1 = 1 and MD2 = 1 are
causes of the Ô¨Åre in the collusive disjunctive scenario discussed above. This is perhaps most
interesting in the case of the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, where in the original disjunctive scenario,
the cause was the conjunction MD = 1 ‚àßL = 1. However, in this case, each arsonist is a
but-for cause of the Ô¨Åre. Although it takes only one match to start the Ô¨Åre, if arsonist 1 does
not drop his match, then arsonist 2 won‚Äôt drop his match either, so there will not be a Ô¨Åre. The
key point is that the arsonists do not act independently here, whereas in the original story, the
arsonist and lightning were viewed as independent.
Interestingly, we can make even the collusion story recursive. Suppose that we add a
variable EC for eye contact (allowing for the possibility that the arsonists avoid each other‚Äôs
eyes), where the equations are such that each drops the match only if they do indeed have
eye contact. Now we are back at a model where each arsonist is (separately) a cause of the
Ô¨Åre (as is the eye contact) according to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, and MD1 =
1 ‚àßMD2 = 1 is a cause according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. We can debate which is the

58
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
‚Äúbetter‚Äù model here. But I have no reason to believe that we can always replace a nonrecursive
model by a recursive model and still describe essentially the same situation.
Finally, it is worth noting that causality is not necessarily asymmetric in nonrecursive mod-
els; we can have X = x being a cause of Y = y and Y = y being a cause of X = x. For
example, in the model of the collusive story above (without the variable EC), MD1 = 1 is a
cause of MD2 = 1 and MD2 = 1 is a cause of MD1 = 1. It should not be so surprising that
we can have such symmetric causality relations in nonrecursive models. However, it is easy
to see that causality is asymmetric in recursive models, according to all the variants of the HP
deÔ¨Ånition. AC2(a) and AC2(am) guarantee that if X = x is a cause of Y = y in (M, u), then
Y depends on X in context u. Similarly, X must depend on Y if Y = y is a cause of X = x
in (M, u). This cannot be the case in a recursive model.
Although the deÔ¨Ånition of causality in nonrecursive models given here seems like the most
natural generalization of the deÔ¨Ånition for recursive models, I do not have convincing exam-
ples suggesting that this is the ‚Äúright‚Äù deÔ¨Ånition, nor do I have examples showing that this
deÔ¨Ånition is problematic in some ways. This is because all the standard examples are most
naturally modeled using recursive models. It would be useful to have more examples of sce-
narios that are best captured by nonrecursive models for which we have reasonable intuitions
regarding the ascription of causality.
2.8
AC2(bo) vs. AC2(bu)
In this section, I consider more carefully AC2(bo) and AC2(bu), to give the reader a sense of
the subtle differences between original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. I start with the example
that originally motivated replacing AC2(bo) by AC2(bu).
Example 2.8.1
Suppose that a prisoner dies either if A loads B‚Äôs gun and B shoots or if
C loads and shoots his gun. Taking D to represent the prisoner‚Äôs death and making the
obvious assumptions about the meaning of the variables, we have that D = (A ‚àßB) ‚à®C.
(Note that here I am identifying the binary variables A, B, C, and D with primitive propo-
sitions in propositional logic, as I said earlier I would. I could have also written this as
D = max(min(A, B), C).)
Suppose that in the actual context u, A loads B‚Äôs gun, B
does not shoot, but C does load and shoot his gun, so the prisoner dies. That is, A = 1,
B = 0, and C = 1.
Clearly C = 1 is a cause of D = 1.
We would not want
to say that A = 1 is a cause of D = 1, given that B did not shoot (i.e., given that
B = 0). However, suppose that we take the obvious model with the variables A, B, C,
D. With AC2(bo), A = 1 is a cause of D = 1. For we can take ‚ÉóW = {B, C} and
consider the contingency where B = 1 and C = 0.
It is easy to check that AC2(a)
and AC2(bo) hold for this contingency, since (M, u) |= [A ‚Üê0, B ‚Üê1, C ‚Üê0](D = 0),
whereas (M, u) |= [A ‚Üê1, B ‚Üê1, C ‚Üê0](D = 1). Thus, according to the original HP
deÔ¨Ånition, A = 1 is a cause of D = 1.
However, AC2(bu) fails in this case because
(M, u) |= [A ‚Üê1, C ‚Üê0](D = 0). The key point is that AC2(bu) says that for A = 1
to be a cause of D = 1, it must be the case that D = 1 even if only some of the values in ‚ÉóW
are set to their values ‚Éów. In this case, by setting only A to 1 and leaving B unset, B takes on
its original value of 0, in which case D = 0. AC2(bo) does not consider this case.

2.8 AC2(bo) vs. AC2(bu)
59
Note that the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition also does not call A = 1 a cause of D = 1. This fol-
lows from Theorem 2.2.3 and the observations above; it can also be seen easily by observing
that if the values of any subset of variables are Ô¨Åxed at their actual values in context u, setting
A = 0 will not make D = 0.
Although it seems clear that we do not want A = 1 to be a cause of D = 1, the situation
for the original HP deÔ¨Ånition is not as bleak as it may appear. We can deal with this example
using the original HP deÔ¨Ånition if we add extra variables, as we did with the Billy-Suzy rock-
throwing example. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, suppose that we add a variable B‚Ä≤ for ‚ÄúB shoots a loaded
gun‚Äù, where B‚Ä≤ = A ‚àßB and D = B‚Ä≤ ‚à®C. This slight change prevents A = 1 from being
a cause of D = 1, even according to the original HP deÔ¨Ånition. I leave it to the reader to
check that any attempt to now declare A = 1 a cause of D = 1 according to the original
HP deÔ¨Ånition would have to put B‚Ä≤ into ‚ÉóZ. However, because B‚Ä≤ = 0 in the actual world,
AC2(bo) does not hold. As I show in Section 4.3, this approach to dealing with the problem
generalizes. There is a sense in which, by adding enough extra variables, we can always use
AC2(bo) instead of AC2(bu) to get equivalent judgments of causality.
There are advantages to using AC2(bo). For one thing, it follows from Theorem 2.2.3(d)
that with AC2(bo), causes are always single conjuncts. This is not the case with AC2(bu), as
the following example shows.
Example 2.8.2 A votes for a candidate. A‚Äôs vote is recorded in two optical scanners B and
C. D collects the output of the scanners; D‚Ä≤ records whether just scanner B records a vote for
the candidate. The candidate wins (i.e., WIN = 1) if any of A, D, or D‚Ä≤ is 1. The value of A
is determined by the exogenous variable. The following structural equations characterize the
value of the remaining variables:
B = A;
C = A;
D = B ‚àßC;
D‚Ä≤ = B ‚àß¬¨A; and
WIN = A ‚à®D ‚à®D‚Ä≤.
Call this causal model MV . Roughly speaking, D‚Ä≤ acts like BH in the rock-throwing example
as modeled by M ‚Ä≤
RT. The causal network for MV is shown in Figure 2.9.
In the actual context u, A = 1, so B = C = D = WIN = 1 and D‚Ä≤ = 0. I claim
that B = 1 ‚àßC = 1 is a cause of WIN = 1 in (MV , u) according to the updated HP
deÔ¨Ånition (which means that, by AC3, neither B = 1 nor C = 1 is cause of WIN = 1 in
(MV , u)). To see this, Ô¨Årst observe that AC1 clearly holds. For AC2, consider the witness
where ‚ÉóW = {A} (so ‚ÉóZ = {B, C, D, D‚Ä≤, WIN }) and w = 0 (so we are considering the
contingency where A = 0). Clearly, (MV , u) |= [A ‚Üê0, B ‚Üê0, C ‚Üê0](WIN = 0), so
AC2(a) holds, and (MV , u) |= [A ‚Üê0, B ‚Üê1, C ‚Üê1](WIN = 1). Moreover, (MV , u) |=
[B ‚Üê1, C ‚Üê1](WIN = 1), and WIN = 1 continues to hold even if D is set to 1 and/or D‚Ä≤
is set to 0 (their values in (MV , u)). Thus, AC2 holds.

It remains to show that AC3 holds and, in particular, that neither B = 1 nor C = 1 is
a cause of WIN = 1 in (MV , u) according to the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. The argument is
essentially the same for both B = 1 and C = 1, so I just show it for B = 1. Roughly
speaking, B = 1 does not satisfy AC2(a) and AC2(bu) for the same reason that BT = 1
does not satisfy it in the rock-throwing example. For suppose that B = 1 satisÔ¨Åes AC2(a)
and AC2(bu). Then we would have to have A ‚àà‚ÉóW, and we would need to consider the
contingency where A = 0 (otherwise WIN = 1 no matter how we set B). Now we need
to consider two cases: D‚Ä≤ ‚àà‚ÉóW and D‚Ä≤ ‚àà‚ÉóZ. If D‚Ä≤ ‚àà‚ÉóW, then if we set D‚Ä≤ = 0, we have
(MV , u) |= [A ‚Üê0, B ‚Üê1, D‚Ä≤ ‚Üê0](WIN = 0), so AC2(bu) fails (no matter whether
C and D are in ‚ÉóW or ‚ÉóZ). And if we set D‚Ä≤ = 1, then AC2(a) fails, since (MV , u) |=
[A ‚Üê0, B ‚Üê0, D‚Ä≤ ‚Üê1](WIN = 1). Now if D‚Ä≤ ‚àà‚ÉóZ, note that (MV , u) |= D‚Ä≤ = 0. Since
(MV , u) |= [A ‚Üê0, B ‚Üê1, D‚Ä≤ ‚Üê0](WIN = 0), again AC2(bu) fails (no matter whether
C or D are in ‚ÉóW or ‚ÉóZ). Thus, B = 1 is not a cause of WIN = 1 in (MV , u) according to the
updated HP deÔ¨Ånition.
By way of contrast, B = 1 and C = 1 are causes of WIN = 1 in (MV , u) according to
the original HP deÔ¨Ånition (as we would expect from Theorem 2.2.3). Although B = 1 and
C = 1 do not satisfy AC2(bu), they do satisfy AC2(bo). To see that B = 1 satisÔ¨Åes AC2(bo),
take ‚ÉóZ = {B, D, WIN } and consider the witness where A = 0, C = 1, and D‚Ä≤ = 0. Clearly
we have both that (MV , u) |= [B ‚Üê0, A ‚Üê0, C ‚Üê1, D‚Ä≤ ‚Üê0](WIN = 0) and that
(MV , u) |= [B ‚Üê1, A ‚Üê0, C ‚Üê1, D‚Ä≤ ‚Üê0](WIN = 1), and this continues to hold if D is
set to 1 (its original value). A similar argument shows that C = 1 is a cause.
Interestingly, none of B = 1, C = 1, or B = 1‚àßC = 1 is a cause of WIN = 1 in (MV , u)
according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. This follows from Theorem 2.2.3; if B = 1 were
part of a cause according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, then it would be a cause according
to the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, and similarly for C = 1. It can also be seen directly: setting
B = C = 0 has no effect on WIN if A = 1. The only cause of WIN = 1 in (MV , u)
according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition is A = 1 (which, of course, is also a cause according
to the original and updated deÔ¨Ånitions).
The fact that causes may not be singletons has implications for the difÔ¨Åculty of determining
whether A is an actual cause of B (see Section 5.3). Although this may suggest that we should

2.8 AC2(bo) vs. AC2(bu)
61
then use the original HP deÔ¨Ånition, adding extra variables if needed, this conclusion is not
necessarily warranted either. For one thing, it may not be obvious when modeling a problem
that extra variables are needed. Moreover, the advantages of lower complexity may be lost
if sufÔ¨Åciently many additional variables need to be added. The modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition has
the beneÔ¨Åts of both typically giving the ‚Äúright‚Äù answer without the need for extra variables
(but see Section 4.3), while having lower complexity than either the original and modiÔ¨Åed HP
deÔ¨Ånition (again, see Section 5.3).
I conclude this section with one more example that I hope clariÔ¨Åes some of the details of
AC2(bu) (and AC2(bo)). Recall that AC2(bu) requires that œï remain true if ‚ÉóX is set to ‚Éóx,
even if only a subset of the variables in ‚ÉóW are set to their values in ‚Éów and all the variables
in an arbitrary subset ‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ of ‚ÉóZ are set to their original values ‚Éóz‚àó(i.e., the values they had in
the original context, where ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx). Since I have viewed the variables in ‚ÉóZ as being on the
causal path to œï (although that is not quite right; see Section 2.9), we might hope that not
only is setting ‚ÉóX to ‚Éóx sufÔ¨Åcient to make œï true, but it is also enough to force all the variables
in ‚ÉóZ to their original values. Indeed, there is a deÔ¨Ånition of actual causality that makes this
requirement (see the bibliographic notes). Formally, we might hope that if (M, ‚Éóu) |= ‚ÉóZ = ‚Éóz‚àó,
then the following holds:
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éów](Z = z‚àó) for all Z ‚àà‚ÉóZ and all ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ ‚äÜ‚ÉóW.
(2.1)
It is not hard to show (see Lemma 2.10.2) that, in the presence of (2.1), AC2(bu) can be
simpliÔ¨Åed to
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ ‚Üê‚Éów]œï;
there is no need to include the subsets Z‚Ä≤. Although (2.1) holds in many examples, it seems
unreasonable to require it in general, as the following example shows.
Example 2.8.3 Imagine that a vote takes place. For simplicity, two people vote. The measure
is passed if at least one of them votes in favor. In fact, both of them vote in favor, and the
measure passes. This version of the story is almost identical to the situation where either a
match or a lightning strike sufÔ¨Åces to bring about a forest Ô¨Åre. If we use V1 and V2 to denote
how the voters vote (Vi = 0 if voter i votes against and Vi = 1 if she votes in favor) and P
to denote whether the measure passes (P = 1 if it passes, P = 0 if it doesn‚Äôt), then in the
context where V1 = V2 = 1, it is easy to see that each of V1 = 1 and V2 = 1 is part of a cause
of P = 1 according to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. However, suppose that the
story is modiÔ¨Åed slightly. Now assume that there is a voting machine that tabulates the votes.
Let T represent the total number of votes recorded by the machine. Clearly, T = V1 + V2 and
P = 1 iff T ‚â•1. The causal network in Figure 2.10 represents this more reÔ¨Åned version of
the story.
In this more reÔ¨Åned scenario, V1 = 1 and V2 = 1 are still both causes of P = 1 according
to the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. Consider V1 = 1. Take ‚ÉóZ = {V1, T, P} and
‚ÉóW = V2, and consider the contingency V2 = 0. With this witness, P is counterfactually
dependent on V1, so AC2(a) holds. To check that this contingency satisÔ¨Åes AC2(bu) (and
hence also AC2(bo)), note that setting V1 to 1 and V2 to 0 results in P = 1, even if T is set to
2 (its current value). However, (2.1) does not hold here: T does not retain its original value of
2 when V1 = 1 and V2 = 0.

Since, in general, one can always imagine that a change in one variable produces some

2.9
Causal Paths
There is an intuition that causality travels along a path: A causes B, which causes C, which
causes D, so there is a path from A to B to C to D. And, indeed, many accounts of causality
explicitly make use of causal paths. In the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition, AC2 involves
a partition of the endogenous variables into two sets, ‚ÉóZ and ‚ÉóW. In principle, there are no
constraints on what partition to use, other than the requirement that ‚ÉóX be a subset of ‚ÉóZ.
However, when introducing AC2(a), I suggested that ‚ÉóZ can be thought of as consisting of the
variables on the causal path from ‚ÉóX to œï. (Recall that the notion of a causal path is deÔ¨Åned
formally in Section 2.4.) As I said, this intuition is not quite true for the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition;
however, it is true for the original HP deÔ¨Ånition. In this section, I examine the role of causal
paths in AC2. In showing that ‚ÉóX is a cause of œï, I would like to show that we can take ‚ÉóZ
(or V ‚àí‚ÉóW in the case of the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition) to consist only of variables that lie on
a causal path from some variable in ‚ÉóX to some variable in œï. The following example shows
that this is not the case in general with the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition.
Example 2.9.1
Consider the following variant of the disjunctive forest-Ô¨Åre scenario. Now
there is a second arsonist; he will drop a lit match exactly if the Ô¨Årst one does. Thus, we add
a new variable MD‚Ä≤ with the equation MD‚Ä≤ = MD. Moreover, FF is no longer a binary
variable; it has three possible values: 0, 1, and 2. We have that FF = 0 if L = MD =
MD‚Ä≤ = 0; FF = 1 if either L = 1 and MD = MD‚Ä≤ or MD = MD‚Ä≤ = 1; and FF = 2
if MD Ã∏= MD‚Ä≤. We can suppose that the forest burns asymmetrically (FF = 2) if exactly
one of the arsonists drops a lit match (which does not happen under normal circumstances);
one side of the forest is more damaged than the other (even if the lightning strikes). Call the
resulting causal model M ‚Ä≤

For the original HP deÔ¨Ånition, we can take the set ‚ÉóZ in AC2, not just in this example, but in
general, to consist only of variables that lie on a causal path from a variable in ‚ÉóX to a variable
in œï. Interestingly, it is also true of the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, except in that case we have
to talk about V ‚àí‚ÉóW rather than ‚ÉóZ because V ‚àí‚ÉóW is the analogue of ‚ÉóZ in the modiÔ¨Åed HP
deÔ¨Ånition.
Proposition 2.9.2 If ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original or modiÔ¨Åed
HP deÔ¨Ånition, then there exists a witness ( ‚ÉóW, ‚Éów, ‚Éóx‚Ä≤) to this such that every variable Z ‚àà
V ‚àí‚ÉóW lies on a causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from some variable in ‚ÉóX to some variable in œï.
For deÔ¨Åniteness, I take X be a variable ‚Äúin œï‚Äù if, syntactically, X appears in œï, even if
œï is equivalent to a formula that does not involve X. For example, if Y is a binary variable,

I take Y to appear in X = 1 ‚àß(Y = 1 ‚à®Y = 1), although this formula is equivalent
to X = 1. (The proposition is actually true even if I assume that Y does not appear in
X = 1 ‚àß(Y = 1 ‚à®Y = 1).) The proof of the proposition can be found in Section 2.10.3.

I repeat the statements of the theorem here (and in the later sections) for the reader‚Äôs conve-

Theorem 2.2.3:
(a) If X = x is part of a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, then
X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original HP deÔ¨Ånition.

X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the updated HP deÔ¨Ånition.

X = x is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original HP deÔ¨Ånition.

(i.e., ‚ÉóX is a singleton).

2.10.2
Proof of Proposition 2.4.6
In this section, I prove Proposition 2.4.6. I start with a simple lemma that states a key (and
obvious!) property of causal paths: if there is no causal path from X to Y , then changing the
value of X cannot change the value of Y . This fact is made precise in the following lemma.
Although it is intuitively obvious, proving it carefully requires a little bit of work. I translate
the proof into statisticians‚Äô notation as well.

66
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
Lemma 2.10.1
If Y and all the variables in ‚ÉóX are endogenous, Y /‚àà‚ÉóX, and there is no
causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from a variable in ‚ÉóX to Y , then for all sets ‚ÉóW of variables disjoint
from ‚ÉóX and Y , and all settings ‚Éóx and ‚Éóx‚Ä≤ for ‚ÉóX, y for Y , and ‚Éów for ‚ÉóW, we have
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów](Y = y) iff (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx‚Ä≤, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów](Y = y)
and
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów](Y = y) iff (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów](Y = y)
(i.e., Y‚Éóx‚Éów(‚Éóu) = y iff Y‚Éóx‚Ä≤ ‚Éów(‚Éóu) = y and Y‚Éóx‚Éów(‚Éóu) = y iff Y ‚Éów(‚Éóu) = y).
Proof:
DeÔ¨Åne the maximum distance of a variable Y in a causal setting (M, ‚Éóu), denoted
maxdist(Y ), to be the length of the longest causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from an exogenous variable
to Y . We prove both parts of the result simultaneously by induction on maxdist(Y ). If
maxdist(Y ) = 1, then the value of Y depends only on the values of the exogenous variables,
so the result trivially holds. If maxdist(Y ) > 1, let Z1, . . . , Zk be the endogenous variables
on which Y depends. These are the parents of Y in the causal network (i.e., these are exactly
the endogenous variables Z such that there is an edge from Z to Y in the causal network). For
each Z ‚àà{Z1, . . . , Zk}, maxdist(Z) < maxdist(Y ): for each causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from
an exogenous variable to Z, there is a longer path to Y , namely, the one formed by adding
the edge from Z to Y . Moreover, there is no causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from a variable in ‚ÉóX to
any of Z1, . . . , Zk nor is any of Z1, . . . , Zk in ‚ÉóX (for otherwise there would be a causal path
in (M, ‚Éóu) from a variable in ‚ÉóX to Y , contradicting the assumption of the lemma). Thus, the
inductive hypothesis holds for each of Z1, . . . , Zk. Since the value of each of Z1, . . . , Zk does
not change when we change the setting of ‚ÉóX from ‚Éóx to ‚Éóx‚Ä≤, and the value of Y depends only
on the values of Z1, . . . , Zk and ‚Éóu (i.e., the values of the exogenous variables), the value of Y
cannot change either.
I can now prove Proposition 2.4.6. I restate it for the reader‚Äôs convenience.

Proposition 2.9.2: If ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original or modiÔ¨Åed HP
deÔ¨Ånition, then there exists a witness ( ‚ÉóW, ‚Éów, ‚Éóx‚Ä≤) to this such that every variable Z ‚ààV ‚àí‚ÉóW
lies on a causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from some variable in ‚ÉóX to some variable in œï.
To prove the result, I need to prove a general property of causal models, one sufÔ¨Åciently
important to be an axiom in the axiomatization of causal reasoning given in Section 5.4.
Lemma 2.10.2
If (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx](‚ÉóY = ‚Éóy), then (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx]œï if and only if
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóY ‚Üê‚Éóy]œï.
Proof:
In the unique solution to the equations when ‚ÉóX is set to ‚Éóx, ‚ÉóY = ‚Éóy. So the unique
solution to the equations when ‚ÉóX is set to ‚Éóx is the same as the unique solution to the equations
when ‚ÉóX is set to ‚Éóx and ‚ÉóY is set to ‚Éóy. The result now follows immediately.
With this background, I can prove Proposition 2.9.2.
Proof of Proposition 2.9.2: Suppose that ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) with witness
( ‚ÉóW, ‚Éów, ‚Éóx‚Ä≤) according to the original (resp., modiÔ¨Åed) HP deÔ¨Ånition. Let ‚ÉóZ = V ‚àí‚ÉóW, let
‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤ consist of the variables in ‚ÉóZ that lie on a causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from some variable in
‚ÉóX to some variable in œï, and let ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ = V ‚àí‚ÉóZ‚Ä≤. Notice that ‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ is a superset of ‚ÉóW. Let
‚ÉóW ‚Ä≤ ‚àí‚ÉóW = ‚ÉóY = {Y1, . . . , Yk}. The proof diverges slightly now depending on whether we
consider the original or modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. In the case of the original HP deÔ¨Ånition, let
yj be the value of Yj such that (M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów](Yj = yj); in the case of the
modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition, let yj be the value of Yj such that (M, ‚Éóu) |= Yj = yj. I claim that
( ‚ÉóW ¬∑ ‚ÉóY , ‚Éów ¬∑‚Éóy, x‚Ä≤) is a witness to ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx being a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the original
(resp., modiÔ¨Åed) HP deÔ¨Ånition. (Recall from the proof of Theorem 2.2.3 that ‚Éóx ¬∑ ‚Éóy denotes
the result of concatenating the vectors ‚Éóx and ‚Éóy.) Once I prove this, I will have proved the
proposition.
Since ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx is a cause of œï by assumption, AC1 and AC3 hold; they are independent of
the witness. This sufÔ¨Åces to prove that AC2(a) and AC2(bo) (resp., AC2(am)) hold for this
witness.
Since ( ‚ÉóW, ‚Éów, x‚Ä≤) is a witness to ‚ÉóX = ‚Éóx being a cause of œï in (M, ‚Éóu) according to the
original (resp., modiÔ¨Åed) HP deÔ¨Ånition, by AC2(a) (resp., AC2(am)), it follows that
(M, ‚Éóu) |= [ ‚ÉóX ‚Üê‚Éóx‚Ä≤, ‚ÉóW ‚Üê‚Éów]¬¨œï.
(2.4)
Since no variable in ‚ÉóY is on a causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from a variable in ‚ÉóX to a variable in
œï, for each Y ‚àà‚ÉóY , either there is no causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from a variable in ‚ÉóX to Y or there
is no causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from Y to a variable in œï (or both). Without loss of generality, we
can assume that the variables in ‚ÉóY are ordered so that there is no causal path in (M, ‚Éóu) from a
variable in ‚ÉóX to any of the Ô¨Årst j variables, Y1, . . . , Yj, and there is no causal path in (M, ‚Éóu)

I Ô¨Årst do the argument in the case of the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition. In this case, ‚Éów must
be the value of the variables in ‚ÉóW in context ‚Éóu; by deÔ¨Ånition, ‚Éóy is the value of the vari-
ables in ‚ÉóY . Thus, ‚ÉóW ¬∑ ‚ÉóY is a legitimate witness for AC2(am). Moreover, since (M, ‚Éóu) |=

Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
Notes
The use of structural equations in models for causality goes back to the geneticist Sewall
Wright [1921] (see [Goldberger 1972] for a discussion). Herb Simon [1953], who won a No-
bel Prize in Economics (and, in addition, made fundamental contributions to Computer Sci-
ence), and the econometrician Trygve Haavelmo [1943] also made use of structural equations.
The formalization used here is due to Judea Pearl, who has been instrumental in pushing for
this approach to causality. Pearl [2000] provides a detailed discussion of structural equations,
their history, and their use.
The assumption that the model is recursive is standard in the literature. The usual deÔ¨Ånition
says that if the model is recursive, then there is a total order ‚™Øof the variables such that X
affects Y only if X ‚™ØY . I have made two changes to the usual deÔ¨Ånition here. The Ô¨Årst
is to allow the order to be partial; the second is to allow it to depend on the context. As the
discussion of the rock-throwing example (Example 2.3.3) shows, the second assumption is
useful and makes the deÔ¨Ånition more general; a good model of the rock-throwing example
might well include contexts where Billy hits before Suzy if they both throw. Although it
seems more natural to me to assume a partial order as I have done here, since the partial order
can be read off the causal network, assuming that the order is partial is actually equivalent to
assuming that it is total. Every total order is a partial order, and it is well known that every
partial order can be extended (typically, in many ways) to a total order. If ‚™Ø‚Ä≤ is a total order
that extends ‚™Øand X affects Y only if X ‚™ØY , then certainly X affects Y only if X ‚™Ø‚Ä≤ Y .
Allowing the partial order to depend on the context ‚Éóu is also nonstandard, but a rather minimal
change.
As I said in the notes to Chapter 1, the original HP deÔ¨Ånition was introduced by Halpern
and Pearl in [Halpern and Pearl 2001]; it was updated in [Halpern and Pearl 2005a]; the
modiÔ¨Åed deÔ¨Ånition was introduced in [Halpern 2015a]. These deÔ¨Ånitions were inspired by
Pearl‚Äôs original notion of a causal beam [Pearl 1998]. (The deÔ¨Ånition of causal beam in [Pearl
2000, Chapter 10] is a modiÔ¨Åcation of the original deÔ¨Ånition that takes into account concerns
raised in an early version of [Halpern and Pearl 2001].) Interestingly, according to the causal
beam deÔ¨Ånition, A qualiÔ¨Åes as an actual cause of B only if something like AC2(am) rather
than AC2(a) holds; otherwise it is called a contributory cause. The distinction between actual
cause and contributory cause is lost in the original and updated HP deÔ¨Ånition. To some extent,
it resurfaces in the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition; in some cases, what the causal beam deÔ¨Ånition
would classify as a contributory cause but not an actual cause would be classiÔ¨Åed as part of a
cause but not a cause according to the modiÔ¨Åed HP deÔ¨Ånition.
Although I focus on (variants of) the HP deÔ¨Ånition of causality in this book, it is not the
only deÔ¨Ånition of causality that is given in terms of structural equations. Besides Pearl‚Äôs
causal beam notion, other deÔ¨Ånitions were given by (among others) Glymour and Wimberly
[2007], Hall [2007], Hitchcock [2001, 2007], and Woodward [2003]. Problems have been
pointed out with all these deÔ¨Ånitions; see [Halpern 2015a] for a discussion of some of them.
As I mentioned in Chapter 1, there are also deÔ¨Ånitions of causality that use counterfactuals
without using structural equations. The best known is due to Lewis [1973a, 2000]. Paul and
Hall [2013] cover a number of approaches that attempt to reduce causality to counterfactuals.

Notes
71
Proposition 2.2.2 and parts (a) and (b) of Theorem 2.2.3 are taken from [Halpern 2015a].
Part (d) of Theorem 2.2.3, the fact that with the original HP deÔ¨Ånition causes are always single
conjuncts, was proved (independently) by Hopkins [2001] and Eiter and Lukasiewicz [2002].
Much of the material in Sections 2.1‚Äì2.3 is taken from [Halpern and Pearl 2005a]. The
formal deÔ¨Ånition of causal model is taken from [Halpern 2000]. Note that in the literature
(especially the statistics literature), what I call here ‚Äúvariables‚Äù are called random variables.
In the philosophy community, counterfactuals are typically deÔ¨Åned in terms of ‚Äúclosest
worlds‚Äù [Lewis 1973b; Stalnaker 1968]; a statement of the form ‚Äúif A were the case then B
would be true‚Äù is taken to be true if in the ‚Äúclosest world(s)‚Äù to the actual world where A is
true, B is also true. The modiÔ¨Åcation of equations may be given a simple ‚Äúclosest world‚Äù
interpretation: the solution of the equations obtained by replacing the equation for Y with
the equation Y = y, while leaving all other equations unaltered, can be viewed as the closest
world to the actual world where Y = y.
The asymmetry embodied in the structural equations (i.e., the fact that variables on the left-
and right-hand sides of the equality sign are treated differently) can be understood in terms of
closest worlds. Suppose that in the actual world, the arsonist does not drop a match, there is
no lightning, and the forest does not burn down. If either the match or lightning sufÔ¨Åces to
start the Ô¨Åre, then in the closest world to the actual world where the arsonist drops a lit match,
the forest burns down. However, it is not necessarily the case that the arsonist drops a match
in the world closest to the actual world where the forest burns down. The formal connection
between causal models and closest-world semantics for counterfactuals is somewhat subtle;
see [Briggs 2012; Galles and Pearl 1998; Halpern 2013; Zhang 2013] for further discussion.
The question of whether there is some circularity in using causal models, where the struc-
tural equations can arguably be viewed as encoding causal relationships, to provide a model
of actual causality was discussed by Woodward [2003]. As he observed, if we intervene to
set X to x, the intervention can be viewed as causing X to have value x. But we are not
interested in deÔ¨Åning a causal relationship between interventions and the values of variables
intervened on. Rather, we are interested in deÔ¨Åning a causal relation between the values of
some variables and the values of otherwise; for example, we want to say that the fact that
X = x in the actual world is a cause of Y = y in the actual world. As Woodward points out
(and I agree), the deÔ¨Ånition of actual causality depends (in part) on the fact that intervening
on X by setting it to x causes X = x, but there is no circularity in this dependence.
The causal networks that are used to describe the equations are similar in spirit to Bayesian
networks, which have been widely used to represent and reason about (conditional) depen-
dencies in probability distributions. Indeed, once we add probability to the picture as in
Section 2.5, the connection is even closer. Historically, Judea Pearl [1988, 2000] introduced
Bayesian networks for probabilistic reasoning and then applied them to causal reasoning. This
similarity to Bayesian networks will be exploited in Chapter 5 to provide insight into when
we can obtain compact representations of causal models.
Spirtes, Glymour, and Scheines [1993] study causality by working directly with graphs,
not structural equations. They consider type causality rather than actual causality; their focus
is on (algorithms for) discovering causal structure and causal inÔ¨Çuence. This can be viewed as
work that will need to be done to construct the structural equations that I am taking as given

72
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
here. The importance of choosing the ‚Äúright‚Äù set of variables comes out clearly in the Spirtes
et al. framework as well.
The difference between conditions and causes in the law is discussed by Katz [1987] and
Mackie [1965], among others.
In the standard philosophical account, causality relates events, that is, for A to be a cause
of B, A and B have to be events. But what counts as an event is open to dispute. There
are different theories of events. (Casati and Varzi [2014] provide a recent overview of this
work; Paul and Hall [2013, pp. 58‚Äì60] discuss its relevance to theories of causality.) A
major issue is whether something not happening counts as an event [Paul and Hall 2013, pp.
178‚Äì182]. (This issue is also related to that of whether omissions count as causes, discussed
earlier.) The As and Bs that are the relata of causality in the HP deÔ¨Ånition are arguably closer
to what philosophers have called true propositions (or facts) [Mellor 2004]. Elucidating the
relationship between all these notions is well beyond the scope of this book.
Although I have handled the rock-throwing example here by just adding two additional
variables (BH and SH ), as I said, this is only one of many ways to capture the fact that
Suzy‚Äôs rock hit the bottle and Billy‚Äôs did not. The alternative of using variables indexed by
time was considered in [Halpern and Pearl 2005a].

case, as Hitchock and I pointed out [Halpern and Hitchcock 2010], this choice of variables is
somewhat problematic because LT and RT are not independent. What would it mean to set
both LT and RT to 1, for example? That the train is simultaneously going down both tracks?

Example 2.4.2 is due to McDermott [1995], who also gives other examples of lack of

The question of the transitivity of causality has been the subject of much debate. As Paul
and Hall [2013] say, ‚ÄúCausality seems to be transitive. If C causes D and D causes E, then C
thereby causes E.‚Äù Indeed, Paul and Hall [2013, p. 215] suggest that ‚Äúpreserving transitivity
is a basic desideratum for an adequate analysis of causation‚Äù. Lewis [1973a, 2000] imposes
transitivity in his deÔ¨Ånition of causality by taking causality to be the transitive closure (‚Äúances-
tral‚Äù, in his terminology) of a one-step causal dependence relation. But numerous examples
have been presented that cast doubt on transitivity; Richard Scheines [personal communica-
tion, 2013] suggested the homeostasis example. Paul and Hall [2013] give a sequence of such
counterexamples, concluding that ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs needed is a more developed story, according to
which the inference from ‚ÄòC causes D‚Äô and ‚ÄòD causes E‚Äô to ‚ÄòC causes E‚Äô is safe provided
such-and-such conditions obtain‚Äîwhere these conditions can typically be assumed to obtain,
except perhaps in odd cases . . . ‚Äù. Hitchcock [2001] argues that the cases that create problems
for transitivity fail to involve appropriate ‚Äúcausal routes‚Äù between the relevant events. Propo-
sitions 2.4.3, 2.4.4, and 2.4.6 can be viewed as providing other sets of conditions for when we
can safely assume transitivity. These results are proved in [Halpern 2015b], from where much
of the discussion in Section 2.4 is taken. Since these deÔ¨Ånitions apply only to but-for causes,
which all counterfactual-based accounts (not just the HP accounts) agree should be causes,
the results should hold for almost all deÔ¨Ånitions that are based on counterfactual dependence
and structural equations.
As I said, there are a number of probabilistic theories of causation that identify causality
with raising probability. For example, Eells and Sober [1983] take C to be a cause of E
if Pr(E | Ki ‚àßC) > Pr(E | Ki ‚àß¬¨C) for all appropriate maximal speciÔ¨Åcations Ki of
causally relevant background factors, provided that these conditionals are deÔ¨Åned (where they
independently deÔ¨Åne what it means for a background factor to be causally relevant); see also
[Eells 1991]. Interestingly, Eells and Sober [1983] also give sufÔ¨Åcient conditions for causality
to be transitive according to their deÔ¨Ånition. This deÔ¨Ånition does not involve counterfactuals
(so the results of Section 2.4 do not apply).
There are also deÔ¨Ånitions of probabilistic causality in this spirit that do involve counterfac-
tuals. For example, Lewis [1973a] deÔ¨Ånes a probabilistic version of his notion of dependency,
by taking A to causally depend on B if and only if, had B not occurred, the chance of A‚Äôs oc-
curring would be much less than its actual chance; he then takes causality to be the transitive
closure of this causal dependence relation. There are several other ways to deÔ¨Åne causality in
this spirit; see Fitelson and Hitchcock [2011] for an overview. In any case, as Example 2.5.2
shows, no matter how this is made precise, deÔ¨Åning causality this way is problematic. Perhaps
the best-known example of the problem is due to Rosen and is presented by Suppes [1970]: A
golfer lines up to driver her ball, but her swing is off and she slices it badly. The slice clearly
decreases the probability of a hole-in-one. But, as it happens, the ball bounces off a tree trunk
at just the right angle that, in fact, the golfer gets a hole-in-one. We want to say that the slice

74
Chapter 2. The HP DeÔ¨Ånition of Causality
caused the hole-in-one (and the deÔ¨Ånition given here would say that), although slicing the ball
lowers the probability of a hole-in-one.
The difference between the probability of rain conditional on a low barometer reading and
the probability that it rains as a result of intervening to set the barometer reading to low has
been called by Pearl [2000] the difference between seeing and doing.
Examples 2.5.1, 2.5.2, and 2.5.3 are modiÔ¨Åcations of examples in [Paul and Hall 2013],
